# Knowledge Base Index


## "Confucian

- [[human-nature-is-good|human-nature-is-good]] — Mencius argues that human nature is inherently good, using the sincere grief during mourning rituals as evidence that moral cultivation through education can develop this innate goodness.

## "Innate Goodness"

- [[human-nature-is-good|human-nature-is-good]] — Mencius argues that human nature is inherently good, using the sincere grief during mourning rituals as evidence that moral cultivation through education can develop this innate goodness.

## Art

- [[zhuangzi-true-painter-anti-art|The True Painter / 眞正的畫者]] — The Zhuangzi story of a painter who achieved perfect spontaneity by removing all conscious thought, embodying Daoist non-action in artistic creation.

## Bao Puzi

- [[confucian-daoist-ease-difficulty-comparison-bao-puzi|Confucianism and Daoism: Ease and Difficulty Comparison / 儒道難易之辯]] — Bao Puzi's systematic comparison of the relative difficulty and ease of Confucian versus Daoist cultivation practices, arguing Daoism is "difficult to know but easy to practice" while Confucianism is "easy to know but difficult to practice."
- [[constellation-fate-determinism-bao-puzi|Constellation-Based Fate Determinism in Bao Puzi / 抱朴子星宿命定論]] — Bao Puzi's argument that human lifespan and potential for immortality are determined by the constellation present at the time of conception, not by divine will.
- [[natural-spontaneity-refutation-divine-creation-bao-puzi|Natural Spontaneity and Refutation of Divine Creation in Bao Puzi / 抱朴子自然與天非造物主說]] — Bao Puzi's philosophical argument using analogies of natural reproduction (lice, midges) to refute the idea that Heaven and Earth deliberately create all things.

## Bodhisattva

- [[bodhisattva-path|The Bodhisattva Path / 菩萨道]] — The Bodhisattva path is a central aspect of Mahayana Buddhism, focusing on the aspiration to attain enlightenment for oneself and others.

## Bodhisattva Stages

- [[bu-chu-yi-sheng-yu-chu|補處 / One Life Remaining Before Buddhahood]] — The bodhisattva stage immediately before full enlightenment, having completed all but the final traces of ignorance.

## Boqian Maoren

- [[liezi-journey-early-return|Liezi's Journey and Early Return / 列禦寇之齊與伯昏瞀人的故事]] — The story of Liezi journeying to Qi but turning back after becoming "startled" by receiving special treatment, illustrating the danger of being sought after by people.

## Buddhism

- [[bodhisattva-path|The Bodhisattva Path / 菩萨道]] — The Bodhisattva path is a central aspect of Mahayana Buddhism, focusing on the aspiration to attain enlightenment for oneself and others.

## Buddhist Cosmology

- [[bu-chu-yi-sheng-yu-chu|補處 / One Life Remaining Before Buddhahood]] — The bodhisattva stage immediately before full enlightenment, having completed all but the final traces of ignorance.

## Buddhist Hermeneutics

- [[xu-fen-buddhist-prologue|序分 / Buddhist Prologue Section]] — The introductory section of Buddhist sutras establishing credibility and context for the main teachings.

## Buddhist Meditation

- [[san-chong-guan-threefold-contemplation|三種觀 / Threefold Contemplation Practice]] — The three meditation methods of empty view, phenomenal view, and middle way observation central to Mahayana Buddhist practice.

## Buddhist Narrative

- [[five-ascetics-seven-śrāvaka-buddhist-narrative|五仙人的故事與七覺支的象徵意義 / 五仙人故事與七覺支象徵意義 / 五仙人の物語と七覚支の象徴的意義]] — The narrative of the five ascetics in the Shengjing, illustrating Buddhist teachings on karma, rebirth, and the development of the seven factors of awakening.
- [[turtle-monkey-past-life-karmic-narrative-shengjing|鱉獼猴前世的業報故事 / 鱉と獼猴の前世因果物語 / 거북이와 원숭이의 전생 업보 이야기]] — The turtle and monkey past-life karmic connection in the Shengjing, illustrating how past-life relationships manifest in the present and the consequences of broken trust.

## Buddhist Parable

- [[nalai-tathagata-story-shengjing|那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人伝と方迹王物語]] — The Buddhist parable of King Fangji enslaved by desire and the ascetic Natai who freed him through teachings on the nature of craving.

## Buddhist Phenomenology

- [[nirodha-samapatti|滅盡定 Nirodhāsamāpatti / Absorption of Cessation]] — A Buddhist meditation attainment where all mental formations cease, enabling practitioners to grant present-world blessings to beings.

## Buddhist Teaching

- [[bird-nali-voice-shengjing|我所鳥的故事 / 我所鳥的故事 / 「我所」鳥の物語]] — The parable of the "Mine-Owner" bird in the Shengjing, illustrating the Buddhist teaching on non-attachment and the futility of possessiveness.
- [[three-poisons-greed-hatred-delusion-buddhist-teaching|三毒的業報與解脫之道 / 三毒的業報與解脫之道 / 三毒の業報と解脱への道]] — Buddhist teachings on the three poisons (贪瞋痴) and their role in binding beings to samsara, as illustrated through multiple narratives in the Shengjing.

## Buddhist Teachings

- [[bihuajing|The "Sad Flower Sutra" / 《悲華經》]] — The "Sad Flower Sutra" is a Mahayana Buddhist text encompassing teachings delivered by the Buddha on various profound subjects.

## Buddhist Text Studies

- [[xu-fen-buddhist-prologue|序分 / Buddhist Prologue Section]] — The introductory section of Buddhist sutras establishing credibility and context for the main teachings.

## Buddhist Texts

- [[four-āgama-commentary-ā-hán-mù-chao-jiě|四阿鋡暮抄解 / Four Āgama Commentary (Ā Hán Mù Chao Jiě)]] — A Buddhist commentary on the Four Āgama text, presenting a systematic analysis of the "Three Factors" framework for Buddhist practice and liberation.

## Buddhist-Medicine

- [[nine-unnatural-deaths-buddhist|Nine Unnatural/Unexplained Deaths / 九橫 / 九横]] — Nine categories of untimely deaths caused by preventable causes such as improper eating, wrong conduct, and association with evil companions.

## Chinese Buddhist Translation

- [[four-āgama-commentary-ā-hán-mù-chao-jiě|四阿鋡暮抄解 / Four Āgama Commentary (Ā Hán Mù Chao Jiě)]] — A Buddhist commentary on the Four Āgama text, presenting a systematic analysis of the "Three Factors" framework for Buddhist practice and liberation.

## Chinese Philosophy

- [[shang-jun-shu-volume-5|Book of Lord Shang Volume 5 / 商君書·卷五]] — The fifth volume of the Book of Lord Shang contains key chapters on military organization, weakening the people, agriculture-war doctrine, and legal administration.
- [[zhuangzi-tianyun-chapter|The Operations of Heaven (Tian Yun) / 天運第十四]] — A philosophical chapter from Zhuangzi exploring natural spontaneity, supreme benevolence, Daoist music, and critiques of Confucian ritual propriety.

## Chinese Classical Texts

- [[guany-yinzi-second-chapter-cosmic-principles|Cosmic Principles in Guany Yinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章的宇宙哲學]] — An exploration of the philosophical concepts in Chapter Two of Guany Yinzi, focusing on the establishment of heaven and earth, the interplay of essence and spirit, and the unity of all things.

## Chinese Cosmology

- [[five-elements-transformation-mind-guanyinzi|The Mind and the Five Elements in Guān Yǐnzǐ Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八章中的心與五行]] — This chapter explores how the mind generates emotions and transformations through desire, and systematically presents the theory of the Five Elements with their generative and conquering cycles.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-two|Guanyinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章]] — Chapter Two of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi explores the cosmological principles of establishing heaven and earth through all things, the interplay of essence, spirit, and the five elements.

## Chinese Ethics

- [[sheng-ren-zhi-dao-sages-way|The Sage's Way / 聖人之道]] — The philosophical principle from Guanyinzi chapter 3 describing how the sage governs through naturalness, humility, and selflessness rather than through personal intervention.

## Chinese Metaphysics

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-essence-spirit|Essence and Spirit (精神) in Guǎn Yǐnzǐ Chapter 4 / 關尹子第四章：精與神]] — A comprehensive analysis of the Daoist concepts of jīng (essence) and shén (spirit) as discussed in Chapter 4 of Guǎn Yǐnzǐ, examining their relationship to the Five Elements and the philosophical principle of "no-self" (wú wǒ).

## Chinese Philosophy

- [[confucian-daoist-ease-difficulty-comparison-bao-puzi|Confucianism and Daoism: Ease and Difficulty Comparison / 儒道難易之辯]] — Bao Puzi's systematic comparison of the relative difficulty and ease of Confucian versus Daoist cultivation practices, arguing Daoism is "difficult to know but easy to practice" while Confucianism is "easy to know but difficult to practice."
- [[constellation-fate-determinism-bao-puzi|Constellation-Based Fate Determinism in Bao Puzi / 抱朴子星宿命定論]] — Bao Puzi's argument that human lifespan and potential for immortality are determined by the constellation present at the time of conception, not by divine will.
- [[fei-gong-against-attack-mozi|Against Attack (非攻) - Mozi's Doctrine of Condemning Aggressive Warfare]] — Mozi's philosophical argument condemning aggressive warfare through progressive analysis of moral inconsistency, demonstrating that large-scale warfare is the ultimate injustice by the same logic used to condemn minor theft.
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-eight-transcendence|Guanyinzi Chapter Eight: The Transcendence of the Dao / 關尹子第八章：道之超越]] — Chapter 8 of Guanyinzi discusses the transcendence of the Dao beyond all categorical distinctions, the transformations of the five elements, and the relationship between mind and things.
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-spirit-five-elements|Guan Yinzi Chapter 4: On Spirit and the Five Elements / 關尹子第四章：論精神與五行]] — Chapter 4 of Guan Yinzi explores the nature of essence (精), spirit (神), ethereal soul (魂), and corporeal soul (魄), examining how the Five Elements correspond to human consciousness and the philosophical implications for understanding life, death, and unity with the cosmos.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-eight|Guanyinzi Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八篇]] — Chapter 8 of the Daoist text Guanyinzi discusses the ineffable nature of the Dao, the five-element transformations, the heart-mind as creator of all things, and the sage's unity with Heaven.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-seven-transformation|Chapter Seven: Transformation (化) in Guanyinzi / 關尹子第七章：化]] — Chapter 7 of Guanyinzi explores the philosophy of transformation (化),阐發道化萬物、聖人任化的深層道理.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-three|Guanyinzi Chapter Three: On the Sage's Non-Action / 關尹子第三章：聖人無為]] — Chapter 3 of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi expounds on the sage's governance through non-action, teaching by example, and the ineffable nature of the Way.
- [[natural-spontaneity-refutation-divine-creation-bao-puzi|Natural Spontaneity and Refutation of Divine Creation in Bao Puzi / 抱朴子自然與天非造物主說]] — Bao Puzi's philosophical argument using analogies of natural reproduction (lice, midges) to refute the idea that Heaven and Earth deliberately create all things.
- [[seeds-of-being-zhongyouji|Seeds of Being (種有幾) / 種有幾]] — The Liezi doctrine of cyclical transformation through which all species interconvert across an intricate chain of metamorphoses.
- [[self-other-illusion-dream-wakefulness-guan-yinzi|Self and Other in Dreams and Wakefulness / 夢中夢外：我與人的虛幻分別]] — An exploration of Guan Yinzi's philosophical argument that the distinction between self and other collapses in dreams, challenging conventional notions of personal identity.
- [[tian-zifang-chapter-zhuangzi|Tian Zifang Chapter / 田子方篇]] — Chapter 21 of Zhuangzi featuring philosophical dialogues on true virtue, spontaneous transformation, and the unity with the Way.
- [[transformation-generation-huasheng-liezi|Transformation and Generation (化生) / 化生]] — The Liezi philosophical principle that all things are generated through transformation in an endless cyclical process.
- [[杞人忧天-qiren-youtian|The Man Who Worried About Heaven Falling (杞人憂天) / 杞人憂天]] — The famous anecdote about unnecessary worry over things beyond one's control, illustrating the Liezi philosophical approach to existential anxiety.

## Confucian Learning

- [[mengzi-moral-cultivation-lost-mind|Moral Cultivation and the Lost Mind in Mencius / 放心与求其放心]] — The concept of "the lost mind" (放心) and the importance of recovering it through learning, as articulated in Mencius's moral philosophy.

## Confucian Philosophy

- [[great-body-small-body-mencius|The Great and Small Bodies in Mencius / 大体与小体]] — Mencius's distinction between the "great body" (mind/heart) and the "small body" (senses), and its implications for moral cultivation.
- [[human-nature-mencius-debate|The Mencian Debate on Human Nature / 孟子人性论辩]] — A philosophical examination of the debate between Mencius and Gaozi regarding the nature of human beings, including key analogies and arguments.

## Confucianism

- [[confucian-daoist-ease-difficulty-comparison-bao-puzi|Confucianism and Daoism: Ease and Difficulty Comparison / 儒道難易之辯]] — Bao Puzi's systematic comparison of the relative difficulty and ease of Confucian versus Daoist cultivation practices, arguing Daoism is "difficult to know but easy to practice" while Confucianism is "easy to know but difficult to practice."

## Contemplation Practice

- [[san-chong-guan-threefold-contemplation|三種觀 / Threefold Contemplation Practice]] — The three meditation methods of empty view, phenomenal view, and middle way observation central to Mahayana Buddhist practice.

## Dao

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-eight-transcendence|Guanyinzi Chapter Eight: The Transcendence of the Dao / 關尹子第八章：道之超越]] — Chapter 8 of Guanyinzi discusses the transcendence of the Dao beyond all categorical distinctions, the transformations of the five elements, and the relationship between mind and things.

## Daoism

- [[confucian-daoist-ease-difficulty-comparison-bao-puzi|Confucianism and Daoism: Ease and Difficulty Comparison / 儒道難易之辯]] — Bao Puzi's systematic comparison of the relative difficulty and ease of Confucian versus Daoist cultivation practices, arguing Daoism is "difficult to know but easy to practice" while Confucianism is "easy to know but difficult to practice."
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-eight-transcendence|Guanyinzi Chapter Eight: The Transcendence of the Dao / 關尹子第八章：道之超越]] — Chapter 8 of Guanyinzi discusses the transcendence of the Dao beyond all categorical distinctions, the transformations of the five elements, and the relationship between mind and things.
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-spirit-five-elements|Guan Yinzi Chapter 4: On Spirit and the Five Elements / 關尹子第四章：論精神與五行]] — Chapter 4 of Guan Yinzi explores the nature of essence (精), spirit (神), ethereal soul (魂), and corporeal soul (魄), examining how the Five Elements correspond to human consciousness and the philosophical implications for understanding life, death, and unity with the cosmos.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-eight|Guanyinzi Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八篇]] — Chapter 8 of the Daoist text Guanyinzi discusses the ineffable nature of the Dao, the five-element transformations, the heart-mind as creator of all things, and the sage's unity with Heaven.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-seven-transformation|Chapter Seven: Transformation (化) in Guanyinzi / 關尹子第七章：化]] — Chapter 7 of Guanyinzi explores the philosophy of transformation (化),阐發道化萬物、聖人任化的深層道理.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-three|Guanyinzi Chapter Three: On the Sage's Non-Action / 關尹子第三章：聖人無為]] — Chapter 3 of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi expounds on the sage's governance through non-action, teaching by example, and the ineffable nature of the Way.
- [[zhuangzi-tianyun-chapter|The Operations of Heaven (Tian Yun) / 天運第十四]] — A philosophical chapter from Zhuangzi exploring natural spontaneity, supreme benevolence, Daoist music, and critiques of Confucian ritual propriety.

## Daoist Cosmology

- [[guany-yinzi-second-chapter-cosmic-principles|Cosmic Principles in Guany Yinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章的宇宙哲學]] — An exploration of the philosophical concepts in Chapter Two of Guany Yinzi, focusing on the establishment of heaven and earth, the interplay of essence and spirit, and the unity of all things.

## Daoist Cultivation

- [[perfect-person-zhiren-zhuangzi|Perfect Person (Zhiren) / 至人]] — The highest ideal in Daoist cultivation - one who attains utmost beauty and joy by wandering freely beyond all transformations and attachments.

## Daoist Meditation

- [[traveling-mind-origin-things-zhuangzi|Traveling Mind to the Origin of Things / 遊心於物之初]] — Laozi's teaching in Zhuangzi on the mind wandering to the primordial origin of all things, describing the state of ultimate beauty and joy.

## Daoist Philosophy

- [[consciousness-recognition-guan-yin-zi|Consciousness and Recognition in Guanyinzi / 關尹子意識與認識論]] — An exploration of the philosophical concepts of consciousness (識), mind (心), and intention (意) as discussed in Chapter 5 of the Guanyinzi, emphasizing the Daoist path from recognition to wisdom.
- [[five-elements-transformation-mind-guanyinzi|The Mind and the Five Elements in Guān Yǐnzǐ Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八章中的心與五行]] — This chapter explores how the mind generates emotions and transformations through desire, and systematically presents the theory of the Five Elements with their generative and conquering cycles.
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-essence-spirit|Essence and Spirit (精神) in Guǎn Yǐnzǐ Chapter 4 / 關尹子第四章：精與神]] — A comprehensive analysis of the Daoist concepts of jīng (essence) and shén (spirit) as discussed in Chapter 4 of Guǎn Yǐnzǐ, examining their relationship to the Five Elements and the philosophical principle of "no-self" (wú wǒ).
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-two|Guanyinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章]] — Chapter Two of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi explores the cosmological principles of establishing heaven and earth through all things, the interplay of essence, spirit, and the five elements.
- [[heart-death-zhuangzi|Heart Death / 心死]] — The Zhuangzi concept that spiritual death (heart death) is greater than physical death, representing loss of true nature and spontaneity.
- [[self-other-illusion-dream-wakefulness-guan-yinzi|Self and Other in Dreams and Wakefulness / 夢中夢外：我與人的虛幻分別]] — An exploration of Guan Yinzi's philosophical argument that the distinction between self and other collapses in dreams, challenging conventional notions of personal identity.
- [[sheng-ren-zhi-dao-sages-way|The Sage's Way / 聖人之道]] — The philosophical principle from Guanyinzi chapter 3 describing how the sage governs through naturalness, humility, and selflessness rather than through personal intervention.
- [[tian-zifang-chapter-zhuangzi|Tian Zifang Chapter / 田子方篇]] — Chapter 21 of Zhuangzi featuring philosophical dialogues on true virtue, spontaneous transformation, and the unity with the Way.
- [[zhuangzi-true-painter-anti-art|The True Painter / 眞正的畫者]] — The Zhuangzi story of a painter who achieved perfect spontaneity by removing all conscious thought, embodying Daoist non-action in artistic creation.

## Five Elements

- [[five-elements-transformation-mind-guanyinzi|The Mind and the Five Elements in Guān Yǐnzǐ Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八章中的心與五行]] — This chapter explores how the mind generates emotions and transformations through desire, and systematically presents the theory of the Five Elements with their generative and conquering cycles.

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-spirit-five-elements|Guan Yinzi Chapter 4: On Spirit and the Five Elements / 關尹子第四章：論精神與五行]] — Chapter 4 of Guan Yinzi explores the nature of essence (精), spirit (神), ethereal soul (魂), and corporeal soul (魄), examining how the Five Elements correspond to human consciousness and the philosophical implications for understanding life, death, and unity with the cosmos.

## Mencius

- [[great-body-small-body-mencius|The Great and Small Bodies in Mencius / 大体与小体]] — Mencius's distinction between the "great body" (mind/heart) and the "small body" (senses), and its implications for moral cultivation.
- [[human-nature-mencius-debate|The Mencian Debate on Human Nature / 孟子人性论辩]] — A philosophical examination of the debate between Mencius and Gaozi regarding the nature of human beings, including key analogies and arguments.
- [[mengzi-moral-cultivation-lost-mind|Moral Cultivation and the Lost Mind in Mencius / 放心与求其放心]] — The concept of "the lost mind" (放心) and the importance of recovering it through learning, as articulated in Mencius's moral philosophy.

## Mind And Emotions

- [[five-elements-transformation-mind-guanyinzi|The Mind and the Five Elements in Guān Yǐnzǐ Chapter 8 / 關尹子第八章中的心與五行]] — This chapter explores how the mind generates emotions and transformations through desire, and systematically presents the theory of the Five Elements with their generative and conquering cycles.

## Mohist Philosophy

- [[fei-gong-against-attack-mozi|Against Attack (非攻) - Mozi's Doctrine of Condemning Aggressive Warfare]] — Mozi's philosophical argument condemning aggressive warfare through progressive analysis of moral inconsistency, demonstrating that large-scale warfare is the ultimate injustice by the same logic used to condemn minor theft.

## Moral Philosophy

- [[fei-gong-against-attack-mozi|Against Attack (非攻) - Mozi's Doctrine of Condemning Aggressive Warfare]] — Mozi's philosophical argument condemning aggressive warfare through progressive analysis of moral inconsistency, demonstrating that large-scale warfare is the ultimate injustice by the same logic used to condemn minor theft.

## Mozi

- [[fei-gong-against-attack-mozi|Against Attack (非攻) - Mozi's Doctrine of Condemning Aggressive Warfare]] — Mozi's philosophical argument condemning aggressive warfare through progressive analysis of moral inconsistency, demonstrating that large-scale warfare is the ultimate injustice by the same logic used to condemn minor theft.

## Natural Spontaneity

- [[zhuangzi-tianyun-chapter|The Operations of Heaven (Tian Yun) / 天運第十四]] — A philosophical chapter from Zhuangzi exploring natural spontaneity, supreme benevolence, Daoist music, and critiques of Confucian ritual propriety.

## Non-Action

- [[zhuangzi-true-painter-anti-art|The True Painter / 眞正的畫者]] — The Zhuangzi story of a painter who achieved perfect spontaneity by removing all conscious thought, embodying Daoist non-action in artistic creation.

## Shang Yang

- [[shang-jun-shu-volume-5|Book of Lord Shang Volume 5 / 商君書·卷五]] — The fifth volume of the Book of Lord Shang contains key chapters on military organization, weakening the people, agriculture-war doctrine, and legal administration.

## Spiritual Progress

- [[bu-chu-yi-sheng-yu-chu|補處 / One Life Remaining Before Buddhahood]] — The bodhisattva stage immediately before full enlightenment, having completed all but the final traces of ignorance.

## Spontaneity

- [[zhuangzi-true-painter-anti-art|The True Painter / 眞正的畫者]] — The Zhuangzi story of a painter who achieved perfect spontaneity by removing all conscious thought, embodying Daoist non-action in artistic creation.

## Sun Shu'Ao

- [[sun-shuao-equanimity-zhuangzi|Sun Shu'ao's Equanimity / 孫叔敖之無憂]] — The story of Sun Shu'ao who served three times as prime minister without joy and left three times without sorrow, embodying Daoist detachment from official rank.

## Sutra Structure

- [[xu-fen-buddhist-prologue|序分 / Buddhist Prologue Section]] — The introductory section of Buddhist sutras establishing credibility and context for the main teachings.

## Taoist Cosmology

- [[seeds-of-being-zhongyouji|Seeds of Being (種有幾) / 種有幾]] — The Liezi doctrine of cyclical transformation through which all species interconvert across an intricate chain of metamorphoses.
- [[transformation-generation-huasheng-liezi|Transformation and Generation (化生) / 化生]] — The Liezi philosophical principle that all things are generated through transformation in an endless cyclical process.

## Taoist Philosophy

- [[guany-yinzi-second-chapter-cosmic-principles|Cosmic Principles in Guany Yinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章的宇宙哲學]] — An exploration of the philosophical concepts in Chapter Two of Guany Yinzi, focusing on the establishment of heaven and earth, the interplay of essence and spirit, and the unity of all things.

## Three Factors Doctrine

- [[four-āgama-commentary-ā-hán-mù-chao-jiě|四阿鋡暮抄解 / Four Āgama Commentary (Ā Hán Mù Chao Jiě)]] — A Buddhist commentary on the Four Āgama text, presenting a systematic analysis of the "Three Factors" framework for Buddhist practice and liberation.

## Threefold Truth

- [[san-chong-guan-threefold-contemplation|三種觀 / Threefold Contemplation Practice]] — The three meditation methods of empty view, phenomenal view, and middle way observation central to Mahayana Buddhist practice.

- [[zhuangzi-tianyun-chapter|The Operations of Heaven (Tian Yun) / 天運第十四]] — A philosophical chapter from Zhuangzi exploring natural spontaneity, supreme benevolence, Daoist music, and critiques of Confucian ritual propriety.

## Tian Zifang

## Wenshi Zhenjing

- [[guanyinzi-chapter-two|Guanyinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章]] — Chapter Two of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi explores the cosmological principles of establishing heaven and earth through all things, the interplay of essence, spirit, and the five elements.

## Zhuangzi

- [[heart-death-zhuangzi|Heart Death / 心死]] — The Zhuangzi concept that spiritual death (heart death) is greater than physical death, representing loss of true nature and spontaneity.
- [[liezi-journey-early-return|Liezi's Journey and Early Return / 列禦寇之齊與伯昏瞀人的故事]] — The story of Liezi journeying to Qi but turning back after becoming "startled" by receiving special treatment, illustrating the danger of being sought after by people.
- [[perfect-person-zhiren-zhuangzi|Perfect Person (Zhiren) / 至人]] — The highest ideal in Daoist cultivation - one who attains utmost beauty and joy by wandering freely beyond all transformations and attachments.
- [[sun-shuao-equanimity-zhuangzi|Sun Shu'ao's Equanimity / 孫叔敖之無憂]] — The story of Sun Shu'ao who served three times as prime minister without joy and left three times without sorrow, embodying Daoist detachment from official rank.
- [[tian-zifang-chapter-zhuangzi|Tian Zifang Chapter / 田子方篇]] — Chapter 21 of Zhuangzi featuring philosophical dialogues on true virtue, spontaneous transformation, and the unity with the Way.
- [[traveling-mind-origin-things-zhuangzi|Traveling Mind to the Origin of Things / 遊心於物之初]] — Laozi's teaching in Zhuangzi on the mind wandering to the primordial origin of all things, describing the state of ultimate beauty and joy.
- [[zhuangzi-tianyun-chapter|The Operations of Heaven (Tian Yun) / 天運第十四]] — A philosophical chapter from Zhuangzi exploring natural spontaneity, supreme benevolence, Daoist music, and critiques of Confucian ritual propriety.
- [[zhuangzi-true-painter-anti-art|The True Painter / 眞正的畫者]] — The Zhuangzi story of a painter who achieved perfect spontaneity by removing all conscious thought, embodying Daoist non-action in artistic creation.

## Abhidharma

- [[three-dharmas-framework-buddhism|Three Dharmas / 三法 / 三法]] — A Buddhist doctrinal framework classifying all phenomena into three categories: virtue (德), evil (惡), and basis (依), each containing three subcategories.

## Adversity

- [[born-in-adversity-die-in-comfort|Born in Adversity, Die in Comfort / 生于忧患，死于安乐]] — Mencius' famous philosophical statement on how hardship builds character and virtue, while comfort leads to moral decline.
- [[生子憂患死於安樂|Birth through Suffering, Death through Comfort / 生子憂患死於安樂]] — A philosophical concept from Liezi expressing that hardship produces growth while comfort leads to decline.

## Agama

- [[changahan-jing|Chang Ahan Jing (Long Discourses of the Buddha) / 長阿含經]] — The Dīrgha-āgama, the longest collection of the Buddha's long discourses in early Buddhist scripture, compiled in four sections and five recitations.
- [[seven-buddhas-current-kalpa|Seven Buddhas of the Current Kalpa / 七佛]] — The seven Buddhas who have appeared in the current kalpa, from Vipassin to Shakyamuni.
- [[thirty-seven-bodhiparamitas-an-shigao|Thirty-Seven Factors of Enlightenment / 三十七道品]] — A systematic classification of Buddhist practice teachings organized in progressive numerical categories (1-10), as presented in An Shigao's translation of the Long Discourses.

## Agama-Buddhism

- [[four-contemplations-buddhist-practice-ahang|Four Contemplations / 四事 / 四つの観法]] — Buddhist contemplative practice of observing body, sensations, mind, and dharma in oneself and others, as taught in the Ahang正行經.
- [[mind-stillness-buddhist-meditation-stone-water|Mind Stillness Analogies in Buddhist Meditation / 持心如石與心如流水 / 仏教瞑想における心の静止の比喩]] — Two key meditation analogies—holding the mind like stone and the mind flowing like water—from the Ahang正行經 for achieving mental stability and understanding impermanence.

## Agama-Literature

- [[an-shigao-translated-agama-zhengxing-sutra|Fo Shuo Ahán Zhèng Xíng Jīng (The Sutra of Proper Conduct in Āgama) / 佛說阿含正行經]] — An Eastern Han translation by An Shigao presenting core Buddhist teachings on proper conduct, the five thieves, and mental cultivation.

## Agama-Sutra

- [[agama-explanation-twelve-links-dependent-origination|Āgama Explanation of Twelve Links of Dependent Origination / 阿含口解十二因緣經]] — A Han Dynasty Buddhist sutra on the Twelve Links of Dependent Origination, translated by An Shigao with unique inner-outer correlation teachings and karmic analysis.

## Agricultural-Policy

- [[lai-min-attracting-people|Attracting People Policy (徠民) / 徠民政策]] — Shang Yang's policy to attract settlers from other states to cultivate Qin's wastelands, addressing population scarcity through land incentives.

## Agricultural-Warfare

- [[nong-zhan-agricultural-war|Agricultural War Doctrine / 農戰]] — A Legalist doctrine from the Book of Lord Shang emphasizing the unification of agricultural production and military service as the twin pillars of state power.

## Agriculture

- [[agricultural-foundation|Agricultural Foundation / 农本思想]] — Mencius's governance principle that agricultural development is fundamental to state prosperity

## Alchemy

- [[baopuzi-yellow-white-alchemy|Baopuzi on Yellow and White Alchemy / 抱朴子論黃白之術]] — Volume 16 of Ge Hong's Baopuzi presents a systematic exposition of 黄白术 (transmutation alchemy), defending its legitimacy and providing detailed alchemical formulas for creating gold and silver.

## Altruism

- [[dana-paramita-bodhisattva-giving|Dāna Pāramitā / 檀波羅蜜 / 檀波羅蜜]] — The perfection of giving in Mahayana Buddhism, exemplified by bodhisattvas who sacrifice everything for the welfare of beings.

## Ambition

- [[zhuangzi-shanxing-chapter|Shanxing (Rectifying Nature) Chapter in Zhuangzi / 莊子·繕性]] — A chapter discussing the rectification of nature through stillness and naturalness, contrasting worldly learning with returning to original spontaneity, and analyzing the historical decline of the Dao.

## An Shigao

- [[七覺意注-(安世高)|Seven Factors of Enlightenment Commentary (An Shigao) / 七覺意注（安世高）]] — An early Chinese commentary by An Shigao on the seven factors of enlightenment within the thirty‑seven bodhiparamitas.
- [[五根五力注-(安世高)|Five Roots and Five Powers Commentary (An Shigao) / 五根五力注 (安世高)]] — Commentary on the Five Roots and Five Powers by the early Chinese Buddhist translator An Shigao, forming part of the systematic exposition of the thirty‑seven bodhipāramitās.

## An-Shigao

- [[agama-explanation-twelve-links-dependent-origination|Āgama Explanation of Twelve Links of Dependent Origination / 阿含口解十二因緣經]] — A Han Dynasty Buddhist sutra on the Twelve Links of Dependent Origination, translated by An Shigao with unique inner-outer correlation teachings and karmic analysis.
- [[an-shigao-seven-places-three-contemplations|An Shigao's Contribution: Seven Places and Three Contemplations / 安世高與七處三觀經]] — The Han dynasty translator An Shigao's systematic introduction of Buddhist meditative analysis through his translation of the Seven Places and Three Contemplations sutra.
- [[an-shigao-translated-agama-zhengxing-sutra|Fo Shuo Ahán Zhèng Xíng Jīng (The Sutra of Proper Conduct in Āgama) / 佛說阿含正行經]] — An Eastern Han translation by An Shigao presenting core Buddhist teachings on proper conduct, the five thieves, and mental cultivation.
- [[an-shigao-translation-methodology|An Shigao's Translation Methodology / 安世高譯經方法]] — The translation techniques and contribution of An Shigao, the seminal Parthian monk who translated over 90 Buddhist texts into Chinese during the Eastern Han dynasty.
- [[changahan-shi-bao-jing|Chang A Han Shi Bao Jing (Long Discourses Tenfold Teachings Sutra) / 長阿含十報法經]] — An early Buddhist sutra presenting systematic teachings from one to ten, translated by An Shigao during the Eastern Han dynasty.
- [[five-aggregates-seven-aspects-an-shigao|Five Aggregates Analyzed Through Seven Aspects / 五蘊七事分析法]] — An Shigao's systematic method of analyzing each of the five aggregates (form, feeling, perception, formations, consciousness) through seven contemplative aspects.
- [[five-roots-five-powers-an-shigao-commentary|Five Roots and Five Powers / 五根五力]] — The Five Roots (信進念定慧) and Five Powers (信力精進力念力定力慧力) as expounded in An Shigao's Han Dynasty Buddhist commentary.
- [[four-contemplations-buddhist-practice-ahang|Four Contemplations / 四事 / 四つの観法]] — Buddhist contemplative practice of observing body, sensations, mind, and dharma in oneself and others, as taught in the Ahang正行經.
- [[four-foundations-mindfulness-an-shigao-commentary|Four Foundations of Mindfulness / 四意止]] — The Four Foundations of Mindfulness (四意止) as interpreted in An Shigao's Han Dynasty commentary on the Satipatthana Sutra.
- [[four-right-exertions-an-shigao-commentary|Four Right Exertions / 四意斷]] — The Four Right Exertions (四意斷) as explained in An Shigao's Han Dynasty commentary on Buddhist meditation practice.
- [[four-types-people-self-other-care-buddhist|Four Types of People: Self-Care and Care for Others / 四等人自護護他]] — A Buddhist classification from the 七處三觀經 describing four types of people based on whether they guard themselves and whether they protect others.
- [[human-desire-to-be-born-sutra|Sutra on Human Desire to Be Born / 人本欲生經]] — A Buddhist sutra on the twelve links of dependent origination, attributed to An Shigao's translation from the Later Han dynasty.
- [[mind-stillness-buddhist-meditation-stone-water|Mind Stillness Analogies in Buddhist Meditation / 持心如石與心如流水 / 仏教瞑想における心の静止の比喩]] — Two key meditation analogies—holding the mind like stone and the mind flowing like water—from the Ahang正行經 for achieving mental stability and understanding impermanence.
- [[prince-mupo-sutra-mu-po-tai-zi|Prince Mupo Sutra / 太子慕魄經]] — A Buddhist sutra about Prince Mupo who remembered past lives, remained silent for 13 years, refused the throne, and became the Buddha, teaching about karmic consequences of excessive mercy.
- [[seven-factors-awakening-an-shigao-commentary|Seven Factors of Awakening / 七覺意]] — The Seven Factors of Awakening (念、择法、精进、喜、轻安、定、捨) as explained in An Shigao's Han Dynasty Buddhist commentary.
- [[thirty-seven-bodhiparamitas-an-shigao-commentary|Thirty-Seven Bodhiparamitas / 三十七品道品 / 三十七波羅蜜]] — A detailed analysis of the thirty-seven practices leading to enlightenment as expounded in An Shigao's commentary on the Yin Chi Ru Jing.
- [[thirty-seven-bodhiparamitas-an-shigao|Thirty-Seven Factors of Enlightenment / 三十七道品]] — A systematic classification of Buddhist practice teachings organized in progressive numerical categories (1-10), as presented in An Shigao's translation of the Long Discourses.
- [[twelve-links-dependent-origination-an-shigao-commentary|Twelve Links of Dependent Origination / 十二因緣 / 十二縁起]] — Analysis of the twelve links of dependent origination as explained in An Shigao's commentary on the Yin Chi Ru Jing.

## Analects

- [[analects-disciples|Disciples in the Analects / 论语弟子群像]] — Notable disciples appearing in Confucius's Analects
- [[analects-xue-er|Learning and Its Practice / 学而时习之]] — The opening chapter of the Analects emphasizing the importance of continuous learning, review, and friendship in personal cultivation.
- [[ba-yi-ceremonial-dance|Ba Yi (Eight Rows) / 八佾]] — The ceremonial dance of eight rows of eight performers, a symbol of ritual order and social hierarchy in ancient Chinese rites.
- [[benevolence|Benevolence / 仁]] — Discusses the foundational role of benevolence in Confucian ethics and its implications for interpersonal relationships and governance.
- [[benevolent-government-ren-zheng|Benevolent Government / 仁政 / 仁政]] — Mencius's political philosophy advocating that rulers should govern through benevolence and moral virtue rather than coercion and punishment.
- [[confucian-ethics|Confucian Ethics / 儒家伦理]] — This article explores the key concepts of Confucian ethics as presented in the Analects, focusing on filial piety and social conduct.
- [[confucian-music-yue|Confucian View of Music (Yue) / 儒家的音乐观（乐）]] — Confucian philosophy on the moral and social functions of music in maintaining ritual order and personal cultivation.
- [[confucian-political-philosophy|Confucian Political Philosophy / 儒家政治哲学]] — Confucius' teachings on governance, social order, and the proper exercise of power
- [[confucian-self-examination|Self-Examination in Confucianism / 三省吾身]] — The practice of daily self-reflection as a fundamental method of moral cultivation in Confucian thought.
- [[confucian-trust-verification|Confucian Trust Verification / 儒家信任验证]] — Examination of trust-building mechanisms in Confucian digital humanities platforms
- [[confucius-analects|Analects of Confucius / 论语]] — A collection of sayings and ideas attributed to the Chinese philosopher Confucius.
- [[confucius-etiquette-xiang-dang|Confucius's Etiquette in Public Life / 孔子乡党礼仪]] — Chapter 10 of the Analects describing Confucius's formal behavior and ritual propriety in various social contexts including village, court, ceremonies, and interactions with different ranks of people.
- [[five-virtues-four-vices|Five Virtues and Four Vices / 五美四恶]] — Confucius's political philosophy outlining five virtues to cultivate and four vices to avoid in governance
- [[governance-virtue-de|Governance through Virtue / 为政以德]] — Confucius's political philosophy emphasizing rule through moral virtue and ethical leadership rather than coercion or punishment.
- [[human-nature|Human Nature / 人性]] — Exploration of the concept of human nature as viewed by Mencius within Confucian philosophy.
- [[junzi|The Role of the Junzi / 君子的角色]] — Examines the characteristics and responsibilities of the Junzi in Confucian philosophy.
- [[knowing-destiny-rites-words|Knowing Destiny, Rites, and Words / 知命知礼知言]] — Confucius's three essential knowledges for becoming a true gentleman in the Analects
- [[knowledge-learning|Knowledge and Learning / 知识与学习]] — Explores the significance of knowledge and learning in Confucian thought and their implications for personal growth and governance.
- [[moral-responsibility|Moral Responsibility / 道德责任]] — Moral responsibility emphasizes the ethical obligations individuals hold towards society and each other.
- [[yan-hui|Yan Hui / 颜回 / 顔回]] — Confucius's most beloved disciple, renowned for his wisdom, virtue, and contentment in poverty
- [[zigong|Zigong / 子贡]] — A prominent disciple of Confucius known for diplomatic skill and commercial success.
- [[zilu|Zilu / 子路]] — A prominent disciple of Confucius known for his boldness and loyalty.

## Ananda

- [[ananda-compiled-scriptures|阿難集經 Ānanda's Compilation of the Buddhist Canon]] — The Buddha's attendant who compiled the sutras after the Buddha's parinirvana, organizing them into the three treasures and four collections.
- [[ten-remembrances-buddhist-meditation|Ten Remembrances / 十念]] — A Buddhist meditation practice involving ten objects of mindfulness, from the Commentary on Classifying Merit.

## Anapana

- [[four-agnas-four-absorptions-buddhist-meditation|四種阿那般那 / Four Ānāpānas / 四つの阿那般那]] — A Buddhist meditation practice involving four types of breath contemplation and mental development stages.

## Anarchism

- [[zhuangzi-thieving-box-qu-qie|Zhuangzi's "Thieving the Box" / 莊子·胠篋]] — A Zhuangzi chapter arguing that preventive measures against petty theft enable great thieves, and that sage teachings are exploited by those who steal states.

## Ancestral-Worship

- [[clan-lineage-system-daxizhuan|The Clan Lineage System in Daxizhuan / 禮記大傳宗法制度]] — The Daxizhuan outlines the "separate branch" (別子為祖) lineage system that governed kinship organization, ancestral worship, and inheritance in ancient Chinese society.

## Ancient China

- [[杞人忧天-qiren-youtian|The Man Who Worried About Heaven Falling (杞人憂天) / 杞人憂天]] — The famous anecdote about unnecessary worry over things beyond one's control, illustrating the Liezi philosophical approach to existential anxiety.

## Ancient China

- [[han-dynasty|Han Dynasty / 汉朝]] — The Han dynasty (202 BC – 220 AD) was the second imperial dynasty of China, marking a golden age of political stability, economic prosperity, and cultural achievement.

## Ancient-China

- [[agricultural-foundation|Agricultural Foundation / 农本思想]] — Mencius's governance principle that agricultural development is fundamental to state prosperity
- [[first-principle-shangjun-shu|First Principle (壹言) / 壹言]] — A foundational concept from the Book of Lord Shang emphasizing the unity of state power and the importance of concentrating national effort on agriculture and military affairs.
- [[four-battle-states-shangjun-shu|Four-Battle States (四戰之國) / 四戰之國]] — A geopolitical concept from the Book of Lord Shang describing states located on multiple frontiers and their strategic imperative to emphasize defensive warfare.
- [[mozi-jie-yong-upper-frugality|Mozi on Frugality (節用上) / 墨子節用上]] — A Mohist chapter advocating efficient governance through eliminating wasteful expenditures and prioritizing practical utility in clothing, shelter, arms, and transportation.
- [[mozi-jie-yong-zhong-frugality-part-2|Mozi - Frugality Part 2 (節用中) / 墨子·節用中]] — A Mohist chapter detailing practical governance principles of the ancient sage kings, emphasizing frugal use of resources across food, clothing, shelter, transportation, military equipment, and burial practices.
- [[mozi-three-debates-music-governance|Mozi's Three Debates on Music and Governance / 墨子三辯]] — A philosophical dialogue in the Mozi examining why sage-kings did not engage in music, and the inverse relationship between musical complexity and governance effectiveness.
- [[mozi-worthy-historians-emperors|Wise Ministers of History / 聖賢履歴]] — Historical examples of unworthy individuals rising to power through virtue, as cited in Mozi's Honor the Worthy chapter.
- [[子貢|Zigong / 子貢]] — A prominent disciple of Confucius known for diplomatic skill and commercial acumen.

## Ancient-Chinese-Government

- [[shang-xian-esteeming-worthy-part-two|Shang Xian (Esteeming the Worthy, Part 2) / 尚賢下]] — Mozi's argument that rulers must appoint the worthy and capable to governance, not favor relatives or the handsome, illustrated through historical examples of sage kings selecting ministers.
- [[three-dukes-gong-sann-gong|Three Dukes (Sān Gōng) / 三公]] — The three highest-ranking officials in ancient Chinese imperial governance, serving as advisors to the Son of Heaven, as described in Mozi's Shang Tong.

## Ancient-Chinese-Law

- [[sangfu-xiaoji-liji|Sangfu Xiaoji (Minor Records of Mourning Dress) / 喪服小記]] — A chapter from the Book of Rites detailing mourning dress regulations, mourning periods, and ritual protocols for various kinship relationships.

## Ancient-Chinese-Military

- [[fenyun-covered-siege-vehicle|Fenyun Covered Siege Vehicle / 轒轀]] — A heavily armored wheeled vehicle used in ancient Chinese siege warfare for crossing water obstacles and breaching defensive works, documented in Mozi's water defense chapter.
- [[mozi-banner-system-siege-defense|Mozi's Banner System for Siege Defense / 墨子旗幟制度]] — Mozi's systematic color-coded banner system for identifying defenders, materials, and military roles during siege warfare.
- [[mozi-preparing-against-elevated-attacks|Mozi on Preparing Against Elevated Attacks / 墨子備高臨]] — A chapter from the Mozi detailing Mohist defensive strategies against enemy siege towers and elevated attack positions, including technical specifications for repeating crossbow carts.
- [[mozi-preparing-against-tunnel-attacks|Preparing Against Tunnel Attacks (Mozi Chapter on Underground Defense) / 墨子備穴]] — A defensive warfare chapter from the Mozi text detailing strategies to detect and counter enemy tunneling attacks under city walls using acoustic detection, smoke熏攻, and structural defenses.
- [[mozi-siege-commands-hao-ling|Mozi's Siege Commands and Military Orders / 墨子號令]] — This chapter from the Mozi text details the command system, military discipline, spy operations, and organizational protocols for siege defense, emphasizing strict hierarchy and reward-punishment mechanisms.
- [[mozi-water-defense-strategies|Mozi's Water Defense Strategies / 墨子備水戰術]] — A chapter from the Mozi detailing defensive preparations and tactical responses against water attacks on fortified cities, including drainage systems, naval organization, and coordinated counterattacks.
- [[mozi-yingdi-ci-qi-observation-military-divination|Qi Observation in Military Divination / 望氣與軍事占卜]] — A Mozi passage describing techniques of observing vital energy (qi) to predict military outcomes, identifying five types of qi that reveal成败吉凶 on the battlefield.

## Ancient-Chinese-Philosophy

- [[mozi-ciguo-refraining-from-excesses|Mozi - Refraining from Excesses (辭過) / 墨子·辭過]] — A chapter from the Mozi examining how ancient sage-kings established proper standards for five essential domains of life—dwelling, clothing, food, transportation, and marriage—contrasting their frugal governance with the excesses of contemporary rulers.
- [[mozi-jie-zang-funeral-simplicity|Mozi's Funeral Simplicity / 墨子節葬思想]] — Mozi's philosophical argument against elaborate funerals and prolonged mourning, advocating simple burial practices based on utilitarian principles.
- [[mozi-three-standards-three-fa|Mozi's Three Standards / 墨子三法]] — Mozi's methodological framework of three standards for evaluating discourse: examination of ancient records, investigation of common experience, and practical application in governance.
- [[qin-guli|Qin Guli / 禽滑釐]] — A devoted disciple of Mozi who served him for three years, later becoming a key figure in Mohist defensive military thought.
- [[shang-tong-upper-unity-mozi|Shang Tong (Upper Unity) / 尚同]] — Shang Tong is Mozi's political doctrine advocating hierarchical unity of thought under proper authority to achieve social order.
- [[shang-xian-exalting-worthy-mozi|Exalting the Worthy (尚賢) in Mozi / 墨子尚賢思想]] — Mozi's doctrine on selecting worthy and capable individuals for government positions, emphasizing merit over kinship as the foundation of good governance.
- [[shang-xian-exalting-worthy|Exalting the Worthy (尚賢) / 尚賢]] — A core Mohist political doctrine emphasizing that rulers should promote individuals of virtue and talent regardless of their social background, making worthy officials the foundation of good governance.
- [[shang-xian-middle-mozi|Shang Xian Zhong (Exalting the Worthy, Part 2) / 尚賢中]] — The second chapter of Mozi's Exalting the Worthy doctrine, presenting the "Three Foundations" theory and historical exemplars of sage kings who achieved governance through elevating worthy ministers.
- [[zhuangzi-daozhi-chapter|Robber Zhi Chapter (盜跖) / 盜跖篇]] — Zhuangzi Chapter 29 presents a fictional dialogue between Confucius and Robber Zhi, featuring radical critiques of Confucian morality, civilization, and social order.

## Ancient-Chinese-Strategy

- [[mozi-water-defense-strategies|Mozi's Water Defense Strategies / 墨子備水戰術]] — A chapter from the Mozi detailing defensive preparations and tactical responses against water attacks on fortified cities, including drainage systems, naval organization, and coordinated counterattacks.

## Ancient-Chinese-Text

- [[liutao-six-secret-strategies|Six Secret Strategies / 六韜 / 六韜]] — An ancient Chinese military treatise traditionally attributed to Jiang Ziya, consisting of six sections covering civil and military governance strategies.
- [[sun-tzu-art-of-war|Sun Tzu's Art of War / 孫子兵法 / 孫氏兵法]] — An ancient Chinese military treatise by Sun Tzu consisting of thirteen chapters covering strategic principles, tactics, intelligence gathering, and the philosophy of warfare.

## Ancient-Chinese-Warfare

- [[mozi-cloud-ladder-defense-beiti|Mozi on Cloud Ladder Defense / 墨子備梯]] — A chapter from the Mozi discussing defensive strategies against cloud ladders in siege warfare, featuring a dialogue between Mozi and his disciple Qin Guli.
- [[mozi-defending-city-walls-beichengmen|Defending City Walls (Mozi) / 墨子備城門]] — A Mohist military defense treatise detailing systematic strategies for city wall protection against twelve siege methods, emphasizing preparation, material readiness, and coordinated defender response.
- [[mozi-preparing-against-tunnel-attacks|Preparing Against Tunnel Attacks (Mozi Chapter on Underground Defense) / 墨子備穴]] — A defensive warfare chapter from the Mozi text detailing strategies to detect and counter enemy tunneling attacks under city walls using acoustic detection, smoke熏攻, and structural defenses.
- [[mozi-yingdi-ci-welcoming-enemy-rituals|Mozi Welcoming Enemy Rituals / 墨子迎敵祠]] — A Mozi chapter describing elaborate ritual protocols for welcoming enemy forces from the four cardinal directions, with corresponding altars, officials, colors, and military preparations.
- [[yun-ti-cloud-ladder|Cloud Ladder / 雲梯]] — An ancient Chinese siege weapon, a heavy mobile tower used to scale city walls, against which Mozi developed sophisticated defensive strategies.

## Ancient-Chinese-Wisdom

- [[hubin-wheelmaking-discourse-zhuangzi|Wheelwright's Wheel-Making Discourse / 輪扁斲輪]] — A philosophical discourse in Zhuangzi where a wheelwright explains to Duke Huan why the words of the sages are merely "dregs" and true wisdom cannot be transmitted through language.

## Ancient-Philosophy

- [[wenzi-text|Wenzi (The Book of Master Wen) / 文子]] — An ancient Daoist text presenting Laozi's philosophy through a dialogue format, attributed to a disciple of Laozi, covering the nature of the Dao, non-action governance, and the ideal sage.

## Anthropology

- [[human-nature|Human Nature / 人性]] — Exploration of the concept of human nature as viewed by Mencius within Confucian philosophy.

## Anxiety

- [[杞人忧天-qiren-youtian|The Man Who Worried About Heaven Falling (杞人憂天) / 杞人憂天]] — The famous anecdote about unnecessary worry over things beyond one's control, illustrating the Liezi philosophical approach to existential anxiety.

## Aranya

- [[lan-ruo|蘭若 / Forest Hermitage Practice]] — 蘭若指佛教修行者居住之寂靜處所，遠離喧鬧，修習頭陀行與止觀禪定

## Argumentation

- [[mozi-fei-ming-against-fatalism|Against Fatalism (非命上) / 墨子非命思想]] — Mozi's philosophical refutation of fatalism and the doctrine of predetermined destiny, arguing for human agency and the Three Standards methodology.
- [[mozi-xiaoqu-logical-methods|Xiaoqu (Minor Selections) - Mozi's Logical Methods / 墨子小取——論理方法]] — A chapter from the Mozi presenting a systematic analysis of dialectical reasoning methods including analogy, parallelism, citing, deduction, and their proper applications.
- [[mozi-xiaoqu-mohist-logic|Mozi Xiaoqu: On Logical Reasoning / 墨子小取：論辯說之法]] — The Xiaoqu chapter of Mozi presents a systematic exposition of Mohist logic, including seven types of reasoning and epistemological principles for philosophical debate.

## Argumentation-Theory

- [[mozi-xiaoqu-logical-methodology|Mozi's Logical Methodology / 墨子逻辑方法论 / 墨子論理方法論]] — A systematic analysis of the logical and dialectical methodology presented in the "Xiaoqu" chapter of Mozi, including the theory of names and realities, argumentation methods, and principles of correct reasoning.

## Arhat

- [[arhat-stages-buddhist-liberation|阿羅漢位 / Stages of Arhat Liberation]] — The systematic classification of Buddhist liberation stages from stream-enterer to fully accomplished arhat, as presented in classical Indian Buddhist texts.
- [[fenbie-gongde-lun|分別功德論 (Fenbie Gongde Lun / Analysis of Merits Treatise)]] — A Buddhist treatise explaining the merits of arhats, the Buddha's disciples, and the compilation of the Three Treasuries.
- [[great-firm-brahman-backstory|The Origin Story of the Great Firm Brāhmaṇa / 大堅固婆羅門緣起]] — A Buddhist sūtra recounting how King Linu's minister Mahākakuda meets the Buddha, attains stream-entry, renounces worldly life, and becomes an arhat, inspiring seven kings, seven thousand brāhmaṇas, and countless laypeople to follow the Dharma.
- [[great-firm-brahman|The Great Firm Brahman / 大堅固婆羅門]] — A jātaka-like narrative about a minister named Humeng who became an arhat through renunciation and taught the Dharma, later identified as a past life of the Buddha.
- [[human-immortal-sutra-ren-xian-jing|Human Immortal Sutra / 人仙經]] — A Mahayana sutra detailing the Buddha's teaching on the rebirth of King Bimbisara through the celestial being Human Immortal.
- [[mahakasyapa-buddhist-disciple-collected-sutras|迦葉 Mahākāśyapa / Mahākāśyapa and the Assembly of 84,000 Arhats]] — The Buddha's senior disciple who led the first council and is portrayed as having been a pratyekabuddha in a previous life.
- [[nirodha-samapatti|滅盡定 Nirodhāsamāpatti / Absorption of Cessation]] — A Buddhist meditation attainment where all mental formations cease, enabling practitioners to grant present-world blessings to beings.
- [[three-types-liberation-buddhist-uppsala|Three Types of Liberation in Buddhist Practice / 佛教三種解脫]] — The classification of liberation into desire liberation, form liberation, and cessation absorption, as discussed in the Three Dharmas Treatise.

## Artificial-Intelligence

- [[gemma-ai-model|Gemma AI Model / Gemma AI 模型 / Gemma AIモデル]] — Google's open-source family of lightweight, state-of-the-art AI models designed for responsible AI development and efficient deployment.
- [[gguf-model-format|GGUF Model Format / GGUF 模型格式 / GGUFモデルフォーマット]] — A unified model file format developed by Georgi Gerganov for efficient storage and inference of quantized large language models.
- [[model-quantization|Model Quantization / 模型量化 / モデル量子化]] — A technique for reducing AI model size and computational requirements by representing model parameters with lower-precision data types.
- [[ollama-framework|Ollama Framework / Ollama 框架 / Ollamaフレームワーク]] — An open-source runtime environment for running large language models locally with quantized GGUF model files.

## Ascetic Teaching

- [[nalai-tathagata-story-shengjing|那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人伝と方迹王物語]] — The Buddhist parable of King Fangji enslaved by desire and the ascetic Natai who freed him through teachings on the nature of craving.

## Ascetic-Practice

- [[defilement-accumulation-in-practice|Defilement Accumulation in Religious Practice / 修行中的烦恼随增]] — Buddhist teaching that external religious practices can still accumulate mental defilements when motivated by pride, desire for respect, jealousy, or other afflictions.
- [[lan-ruo|蘭若 / Forest Hermitage Practice]] — 蘭若指佛教修行者居住之寂靜處所，遠離喧鬧，修習頭陀行與止觀禪定

## Asceticism

- [[niyagrodha-brahma-sutra-discourse|The Discourse on Niḡrodha Brahmā / 佛說尼拘陀梵志經]] — A Mahāyāna sutra depicting the Buddha's discourse with the ascetic Niḡrodha, critiquing false ascetic practices and demonstrating the nature of true Buddhist practice.

## Attachment

- [[nalai-tathagata-story-shengjing|那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人與方迹王的故事 / 那賴仙人伝と方迹王物語]] — The Buddhist parable of King Fangji enslaved by desire and the ascetic Natai who freed him through teachings on the nature of craving.

## Attachment-Suffering

- [[you-ai-gu-sheng-you-you-yin-ai-gu-sheng-kong|From Love Arises Sorrow; From Love Arises Fear / 由愛故生憂 由愛故生怖]] — A canonical Buddhist gatha stating that attachment is the origin of suffering and fear, and liberation comes from detachment.

## Austerities

- [[toutuo-xing|頭陀行 / Dhutanga Practices]] — 頭陀行乃佛教十三種清淨苦行，修習此法可去衣、食、住之貪著，速得解脫

## Authentication

- [[confucian-trust-verification|Confucian Trust Verification / 儒家信任验证]] — Examination of trust-building mechanisms in Confucian digital humanities platforms

## Authenticity

- [[zhuangzi-fisherman-yu-fu|The Fisherman Chapter (Yu Fu) / 莊子·漁父]] — A Zhuangzi chapter featuring a Daoist fisherman who teaches Confucius about authenticity (真), criticizing artificial ritual and social conventions while advocating naturalness and genuine sincerity.

## Authority

- [[chongyan-revered-discourse-zhuangzi|Chongyan (Revered Discourse) in Zhuangzi / 重言（莊子）]] — "Revered Discourse" (重言) — the second of Zhuangzi's three discourse modes, quoting ancient sages and elders to lend authority to philosophical teachings.

## Awakening

- [[seven-factors-awakening-an-shigao-commentary|Seven Factors of Awakening / 七覺意]] — The Seven Factors of Awakening (念、择法、精进、喜、轻安、定、捨) as explained in An Shigao's Han Dynasty Buddhist commentary.

## Balance

- [[cai-yu-bu-cai-zhi-jian|Between Being Useful and Useless / 材與不材之間]] — A Zhuangzi philosophy on navigating between utility and uselessness through alignment with the Dao, avoiding the pitfalls of both extremes.

## Banners

- [[mozi-banner-system-siege-defense|Mozi's Banner System for Siege Defense / 墨子旗幟制度]] — Mozi's systematic color-coded banner system for identifying defenders, materials, and military roles during siege warfare.

## Bao-En-Pin

- [[three-treasures|Three Treasures / 三宝]] — The three foundational refuges of Buddhism—Buddha, Dharma, and Sangha—considered precious objects of veneration and sources of spiritual refuge.

## Baopuzi

- [[baopuzi-chapter-14-golden-elixir-only-path|Golden Elixir as the Sole Path to Immortality / 抱朴子卷十四：金丹為不死之正道]] — Ge Hong's emphatic assertion that only the Golden Elixir (金丹) constitutes true immortality, dismissing rituals, prayers, and supernatural powers as insufficient.
- [[baopuzi-chapter-14-importance-of-seeking-master|The Importance of Seeking a True Master in Baopuzi / 抱朴子卷十四：求师问道]] — Ge Hong's discourse on the rarity of authentic Daoist masters and the necessity of sincere, diligent seeking in cultivation practice.
- [[baopuzi-chapter-14-life-brevity-calculation|Life's Brevity and Urgency in Baopuzi / 抱朴子卷十四：人生短促之歎]] — Ge Hong's vivid calculation of human lifespan, comparing life to a sacrificial animal approaching the slaughterhouse, emphasizing the urgency of cultivation practice.
- [[baopuzi-critique-false-deities-folk-religion|Bao Puzi Chapter 9: Critique of False Deities and Folk Religion / 抱朴子卷九：批判淫祀邪教]] — Ge Hong's philosophical critique of fraudulent religious practices, false deities, and the importance of self-cultivation over external ritual worship.
- [[baopuzi-immortality-debate|Bao Puzi on Immortals / 抱朴子论神仙不死]] — Ge Hong's philosophical defense of immortality through natural transformation and historical evidence.
- [[baopuzi-jiu-dan-nine-elixirs|Nine Elixirs in Baopuzi / 抱朴子九丹]] — Baopuzi Volume 4 details the Nine Elixirs (九丹) — the supreme alchemical formulas for immortality including the Cinnabar Flower, Divine Elixir, Return Elixir, and Cold Elixir, along with the Great Clarity Elixir and Golden Liquid methods.
- [[baopuzi-selecting-daoist-texts|On Selecting Daoist Texts in Baopuzi / 抱朴子論道書之選擇]] — Ge Hong's critical assessment of various Daoist texts including the Daodejing and Zhuangzi, arguing that most are incomplete or misleading, and that true cultivation requires proper guidance rather than mere textual study.
- [[baopuzi-volume-15-grain-fasting|Grain Fasting Methods in Baopuzi / 抱朴子断穀諸法]] — Comprehensive examination of grain fasting (断穀) techniques in Ge Hong's Baopuzi, including dietary restrictions, mineral-based foods, breathing practices, and their effects on health and longevity.
- [[baopuzi-volume-17-mountain-entry|Baopuzi Volume 17: Mountain Entry Arts / 抱朴子卷十七：入山之法]] — Ge Hong's comprehensive guide to Daoist mountain entry practices, including timing, purification rituals, protective talismans, and methods for dealing with mountain spirits and dangerous creatures.
- [[baopuzi-volume-19-daoist-scriptures-catalog|Baopuzi Volume 19: Catalog of Daoist Texts / 抱朴子卷十九道書目錄]] — Ge Hong's comprehensive catalog of over 180 Daoist texts including Three Sovereigns Inner Text, Five Peaks True Form Map, talismans, alchemy scripts, and esoteric practices, as recorded from his teacher Zheng Jun.
- [[baopuzi-yellow-white-alchemy|Baopuzi on Yellow and White Alchemy / 抱朴子論黃白之術]] — Volume 16 of Ge Hong's Baopuzi presents a systematic exposition of 黄白术 (transmutation alchemy), defending its legitimacy and providing detailed alchemical formulas for creating gold and silver.
- [[bu-shang-wei-ben-principle-cultivation|Non-Injury as Foundation in Cultivation / 不傷為本]] — The fundamental principle in Daoist cultivation that protecting the body and preserving qi from damage is the essential foundation for longevity and eventual immortality.
- [[dao-ben-ru-mo-dao-as-root-confucianism-as-branch|Dao as Root, Confucianism as Branch / 道本儒末]] — A philosophical doctrine from Baopuzi asserting that the Dao is the foundation while Confucianism represents its derivative applications.
- [[five-peaks-true-form-map-wuyue-zhenxing-tu|Five Peaks True Form Map / 五嶽真形圖]] — A most sacred Taoist talisman text described in Baopuzi as requiring blood oaths, forty-year transmission cycles, and possessing powers to ward off evil, heal diseases, cross waters, and reveal hidden knowledge.
- [[fuyao-shijie-medicine-timing-baopuzi|Medicine Timing (服藥時節) in Baopuzi / 抱朴子服藥時節]] — The timing principles for taking medicinal elixirs in Daoist alchemy, distinguishing between therapeutic and nurturing medicines.
- [[liang-zuo-shan-two-mountains-immortality-baopuzi|Two Mountains of Immortality in Baopuzi / 抱朴子所述兩座仙山]] — A unique esoteric teaching from Baopuzi describing two mystical mountains where immortality can be obtained through proper cultivation.
- [[shou-yi-zhi-dao-guarding-the-one|Guarding the One (守一之道) / 守一之道]] — A fundamental Daoist practice of maintaining singular focus on the Dao for immortality, protection, and spiritual transformation, as described in Baopuzi.
- [[tai-xi-embryo-breathing-baopuzi|Embryo Breathing (胎息) in Baopuzi / 抱朴子中的胎息之法]] — A detailed examination of the embryo breathing technique (胎息) described in Baopuzi as the supreme method of qi cultivation, enabling practitioners to breathe without using nose or mouth as if in the womb.
- [[wu-zhi-five-fungi-baopuzi|Five Fungi (五芝) in Baopuzi / 抱朴子五芝]] — The classification and effects of five types of fungi (石芝、木芝、草芝、肉芝、菌芝) for immortality in Daoist alchemy.
- [[xuandao-mysterious-way|Xuandao (The Mysterious Way) / 玄道]] — The supreme metaphysical principle "玄" (Xuan/Mystery) discussed in Baopuzi, described as the origin of nature and the great source of all myriad things, transcending physical existence.
- [[zhang-liang-daoist-cultivation-exile|Zhang Liang's Daoist Cultivation and Exile / 张良修道绝谷]] — Zhang Liang (Zhang Liang), famous strategist of the Han founding, abandoned worldly affairs to pursue Daoist cultivation and practices of fasting (绝谷), demonstrating historical evidence for immortality cultivation.

## Benevolence

- [[analects-disciples|Disciples in the Analects / 论语弟子群像]] — Notable disciples appearing in Confucius's Analects
- [[benevolence|Benevolence / 仁]] — Discusses the foundational role of benevolence in Confucian ethics and its implications for interpersonal relationships and governance.

## Benevolent-Government

- [[benevolent-government-ren-zheng|Benevolent Government / 仁政 / 仁政]] — Mencius's political philosophy advocating that rulers should govern through benevolence and moral virtue rather than coercion and punishment.
- [[mengzi-people-heart-minjian|The People's Heart / 民心]] — Mencius' doctrine that legitimate governance depends on winning the hearts of the people through benevolent rule.
- [[mengzi-political-philosophy-benevolent-rule|Mencius's Political Philosophy of Benevolent Rule / 孟子王道政治思想]] — Analysis of Mencius's theory of benevolent government (wangdao) as moral governance
- [[ministerial-advice|Ministerial Advice / 大臣的劝谏]] — The role of ministerial advice is crucial in ensuring benevolent governance and societal harmony.
- [[moral-responsibility|Moral Responsibility / 道德责任]] — Moral responsibility emphasizes the ethical obligations individuals hold towards society and each other.

## Bhiksusamgha-Translations

- [[sarvadana-king-yiqieshi|King Sarvadāna (一切施王) — The Sovereign Who Gave Everything]] — A legendary king who renounced his throne, kingdom, and ultimately his own body for the sake of a single supplicant, exemplifying the ultimate perfection of giving.
- [[sarvadhana-prince-yiqiechi|Prince Sarvadhāna (一切持王子) — The Crown Prince of Universal Possession and Giving]] — A crown prince who gave away royal treasures, elephants, and ultimately his own children to fulfill his vow of universal giving, exemplifying the bodhisattva's renunciation of attachment.
- [[virama-bodhisattva-pusajiamo|Vīrāma (毘羅摩) — Bodhisattva of Giving]] — A minister's son who became chief minister and exemplified the perfection of giving through vast generosity and skillful means.

## Bibliography

- [[baopuzi-volume-19-daoist-scriptures-catalog|Baopuzi Volume 19: Catalog of Daoist Texts / 抱朴子卷十九道書目錄]] — Ge Hong's comprehensive catalog of over 180 Daoist texts including Three Sovereigns Inner Text, Five Peaks True Form Map, talismans, alchemy scripts, and esoteric practices, as recorded from his teacher Zheng Jun.

## Bimbisara

- [[shizi-yue-fo-ben-sheng-jing|The Lion-Moon Buddha Jataka Sutra / 師子月佛本生經]] — A Buddhist Jataka sutra describing how a monkey who received the Five Precepts from an arhat was reborn over multiple lives and eventually became the Buddha Shi Zi Yue (Lion-Moon), with 84,000 golden monkeys receiving bodhisattva predictions.

## Biography

- [[analects-disciples|Disciples in the Analects / 论语弟子群像]] — Notable disciples appearing in Confucius's Analects

## Bodhiparamitas

- [[thirty-seven-bodhiparamitas-an-shigao-commentary|Thirty-Seven Bodhiparamitas / 三十七品道品 / 三十七波羅蜜]] — A detailed analysis of the thirty-seven practices leading to enlightenment as expounded in An Shigao's commentary on the Yin Chi Ru Jing.

## Bodhisattva

- [[miaose-wang-bodhisattva-sacrifice|King Subhūti / 妙色王]] — A past incarnation of the Buddha who sacrificed his son, wife, and body to obtain the Dharma teaching on attachment.
- [[sarvadana-king-yiqieshi|King Sarvadāna (一切施王) — The Sovereign Who Gave Everything]] — A legendary king who renounced his throne, kingdom, and ultimately his own body for the sake of a single supplicant, exemplifying the ultimate perfection of giving.
- [[sarvadhana-prince-yiqiechi|Prince Sarvadhāna (一切持王子) — The Crown Prince of Universal Possession and Giving]] — A crown prince who gave away royal treasures, elephants, and ultimately his own children to fulfill his vow of universal giving, exemplifying the bodhisattva's renunciation of attachment.
- [[shan-zi-sutra-filial-bodhisattva|The Śhanzi Sutra / 佛說睒子經]] — A Buddhist Jataka tale about a bodhisattva named Shan Zi who demonstrates supreme filial piety by being born as the son of blind parents, and whose accidental death by a king's arrow leads to a miraculous resurrection.
- [[virama-bodhisattva-pusajiamo|Vīrāma (毘羅摩) — Bodhisattva of Giving]] — A minister's son who became chief minister and exemplified the perfection of giving through vast generosity and skillful means.
- [[void-seal-bodhisattva|虛空印菩薩 / Void Seal Bodhisattva (Xūkōng Yìn Púsà)]] — A bodhisattva in Mahāyāna Buddhism who received the teaching of all-knowledge dhāraṇī and later became the Lotus-Up Tathāgata in the Lotus World.
- [[yue-ming-tongzi-moonlight-youth-bodhisattva-sutra|Moonlight Youth (月明童子) — Protagonist of the Moonlight Bodhisattva Sutra / 月明童子——《月光菩薩經》的主人公]] — The Moonlight Youth (月明童子) is a clean-minded young man addressed by the Buddha, symbolizing purity of mind in the sutra.

## Bodhisattva-Compassion

- [[rabbit-king-self-sacrifice-hermit|Rabbit King Offering to the Hermit (兔王捨身供奉梵志) - The Self-Sacrifice of a Righteous Rabbit]] — The Jataka story of a rabbit king who, having learned the Dharma from a brahmana, offered his own body as fuel for a fire to feed the ascetic during a time of famine.
- [[throw-body-feed-tigers-jataka|Throw Body to Feed Tigers (投身飼虎) - Jataka Tale of Prince Mahāsattva]] — The Jataka story of Prince Mahāsattva who sacrificed his body to feed seven starving tiger cubs, a paradigm of ultimate bodhisattva compassion.

## Bodhisattva-Giving

- [[king-sibi-pigeon-sacrifice|King Śibi Saves the Pigeon (尸毘王救鴿) - The Great Sacrifice of Flesh for Life]] — The classic Jataka tale of King Śibi who cut off his own flesh to save a pigeon from a hawk, exemplifying the perfection of giving in Buddhism.

## Bodhisattva-Incarnation

- [[ding-sheng-wang-king-caitanya-dhrtarastra|King Ding Sheng (Ding Sheng Wang) / 頂生王]] — King Ding Sheng is a bodhisattva incarnation who was born from his father's crown, practiced generous giving, conquered the four continents, and ascended through the thirty-three heavens, illustrating Buddhist teachings on merit and the dangers of greed.

## Bodhisattva-Narrative

- [[bodhisattva-sacrifice-king-changshou|King Changshou's Sacrifice / 長壽王的犧牲 / 長寿王の犠牲]] — The narrative archetype of King Changshou, a bodhisattva who sacrifices his kingdom and life rather than wage war, embodying Buddhist non-retaliation ideals.

## Bodhisattva-Path

- [[foshuo-pusa-benxing-jing|Bodhisattva Practice Sutra / 佛說菩薩本行經]] — A Buddhist sutra (3 volumes) depicting Jataka tales of past Buddha lives, emphasizing the bodhisattva path through charity, sacrifice, and perseverance.
- [[fuli-prince-fortune-power-cause-effect-sutra|Fuli Prince Sutra on Merit and Fortune / 福力太子因緣經]] — A Mahayana sutra teaching that cultivating merit and fortune surpasses all other virtues, featuring Prince Fuli's story of ascent to Cakravartin kingship through supreme generosity.

## Bodhisattva-Practice

- [[bao-en-pin|報恩品 / The Chapter on Repaying Kindness (Bao'en Pin)]] — A major chapter of the Mahāyāna Sūtra on the Cultivation of the Mind-Ground, systematically explaining the Buddhist doctrine of repaying four kinds of kindness.
- [[compassionate-dragon-king-naga|Compassionate Dragon King (慈心龍王) — Buddhist Narrative of Non-Retaliation / 慈心龍王消伏怨害緣起]] — A Jataka tale depicting a dragon king who conquers the golden-winged garuda through loving-kindness and non-retaliation, exemplifying the power of compassion over violence.
- [[da-cheng-ben-sheng-xin-di-guan-jing|大乘本生心地觀經 / Mahāyāna Sūtra on the Cultivation of the Mind-Ground]] — A major Mahāyāna Buddhist sutra translated into Chinese during the Tang dynasty, teaching the cultivation of the mind-ground and repaying the four kinds of kindness.
- [[da-cheng-li-wen-zi-pu-guang-ming-zang-jing|Mahāyāna Sutra of Universal Light Treasury Transcending Words / 大乘離文字普光明藏經]] — A Mahāyāna Buddhist sutra translated by Divākara during the Tang Dynasty, teaching what bodhisattvas should abandon and realize.
- [[dana-paramita-bodhisattva-giving|Dāna Pāramitā / 檀波羅蜜 / 檀波羅蜜]] — The perfection of giving in Mahayana Buddhism, exemplified by bodhisattvas who sacrifice everything for the welfare of beings.
- [[dayi-jing-great-meaning-sutra|Dayi Jing — The Sutra of the Great Meaning / 大意經]] — A Buddhist narrative sutra from the Song Dynasty describing the bodhisattva Dayi's vow to aid all beings, his quest for seven treasures, and his encounters with four jeweled cities before becoming a Buddha.
- [[deguang-taizi-jing-sutra|Sutra of Prince Deguang / 德光太子經]] — A Buddhist sutra recounting Prince Deguang's renunciation of royal comforts and his quest for Buddhist enlightenment, featuring teachings on the four pure practices and warnings against贪欲 and放逸.
- [[entry-all-knowledge-dharani-gate|入一切種智行陀羅尼門 / Entry into the All-Knowledge Dhāraṇī Gate]] — A profound Mahāyāna dhāraṇī practice for entering complete wisdom, enabling practitioners to obtain countless samādhis,Store dhāraṇīs, and achieve supreme enlightenment.
- [[liang-she-fa-two-grabbing-methods|Two Grabbing Methods / 二攝法 / 二攝法]] — The Buddhist methodology combining material giving (財法) with Dharma teaching (法施) to benefit sentient beings through both physical and spiritual means.
- [[prince-sudana-sutta-bodhisattva-generosity|Prince Sudāna's Story / 太子須大拏經]] — A Jātaka tale depicting Prince Sudāna's practice of dāna pāramitā through extreme acts of charitable giving, including the white elephant, treasury, and his own children.
- [[prince-sujata-story-bodhisattva-sacrifice|The Story of Prince Sūjāta (須闍提太子) - Buddhist Narrative of Self-Sacrifice]] — The narrative of Prince Sūjāta who offered his own flesh to feed his starving parents, serving as the central exemplum of filial piety and compassionate self-sacrifice in Mahayana Buddhism.
- [[si-yuan-four-vows-bodhisattva-practice-yue-ming-jing|Four Vows (四願) in the Moonlight Bodhisattva Sutra / 月明童子經四願——菩薩修行之根本誓願]] — The Four Vows are the fundamental commitments bodhisattvas make for spiritual practice, encompassing skillful means, spiritual friendship, giving, and dharma practice.
- [[thousand-lamp-body-offering-bodhisattva-practice|The King Who Lit a Thousand Lamps / 以身燃千燈之王]] — The Buddhist narrative of a king who sacrificed his own flesh to light a thousand lamps as the ultimate act of dedication to the Dharma.

## Bodhisattva-Realm

- [[lotus-world-buddhist-cosmology|蓮華世界 / Lotus World (Liánhuá Shìjiè)]] — A pure land in Buddhist cosmology described in the Mahāyāna sutras, characterized by lotus imagery, jeweled trees, and spiritual refinement.

## Bodhisattva-Sacrifice

- [[bodhisattva-throw-body-feed-tiger-stupa-fasheng|The Sutra of Bodhisattva Throwing His Body to Feed the Hungry Tiger and the Stupa's Origin / 菩薩投身餧餓虎起塔因緣經]] — A Buddhist sutra describing Prince Chandana's self-sacrifice to feed seven hungry tiger cubs, the subsequent stupa's miraculous healing powers, and its translator Fasheng of Northern Liang Gaochang.
- [[miaose-wang-yinyuan-jing|King Subhūti's Cause and Conditions Sutra / 妙色王因緣經]] — A Buddhist sutra depicting King Subhūti's supreme sacrifice of his son, wife, and body to obtain the Dharma teaching on attachment.

## Bodhisattva-Virtues

- [[non-retaliation-buddhist-teaching|Non-Retaliation in Buddhist Teaching / 不報怨 / 仏教における報復禁止の教え]] — The Buddhist doctrine of abstaining from vengeance and returning harm with harm, exemplified in the story of King Changshou.

## Body-And-Spirit

- [[zhili-qixing-deformed-ones-zhuangzi|The Deformed Ones / 支離其形]] — Zhuangzi's celebration of physically deformed individuals who, excluded from worldly usefulness, survive and prosper—the paradox of form versus spirit in Daoist philosophy.

## Body-Divinities

- [[san-shi-shen-three-corpses|Three Corpses / 三尸神]] — The Three Corpses (三尸神) are celestial observers dwelling within the human body who record sins and report them to heaven during the Gengshen day, playing a central role in Daoist karmic surveillance systems.

## Body-Mind

- [[xing-hai-nei-wai|Inside and Outside the Physical Form / 形骸內外]] — A philosophical distinction in Zhuangzi between the physical body (form) and inner virtue, exemplified by deformed sages whose outer appearance differs from their inner perfection.

## Book-Of-Liji

- [[four-mourning-principles-liji-sangfu-sizhi|Four Mourning Principles (Sangfu Sizhi) / 喪服四制]] — A Confucian framework from the Book of Rites categorizing mourning rituals into four principles—grace, righteousness, propriety, and adaptability—based on benevolence, duty, ceremony, and wisdom.

## Book-Of-Lord-Shang

- [[nong-zhan-agricultural-war|Agricultural War Doctrine / 農戰]] — A Legalist doctrine from the Book of Lord Shang emphasizing the unification of agricultural production and military service as the twin pillars of state power.

## Book-Of-Rites

- [[cheng-qing-er-li-wen-ritual-emotion-alignment|Establishing Ritual Based on Emotion / 稱情而立文]] — A Confucian ritual principle that proper ceremonial forms should be established according to genuine human emotions and feelings, serving as external expressions of internal grief.
- [[clan-lineage-system-daxizhuan|The Clan Lineage System in Daxizhuan / 禮記大傳宗法制度]] — The Daxizhuan outlines the "separate branch" (別子為祖) lineage system that governed kinship organization, ancestral worship, and inheritance in ancient Chinese society.
- [[confucius-at-leisure-liji|Confucius at Leisure / 孔子閒居]] — A Confucian dialogue between Confucius and Zixia on the qualities of an ideal ruler, exploring the Five Arrivals, Three "Not-Haves," and the Three Selflessnesses.
- [[five-priorities-governance-daxizhuan|The Five Priorities of Governance in Daxizhuan / 禮記大傳人道五端]] — The Daxizhuan chapter of the Book of Rites outlines five governance priorities:亲近、报功、举贤、使能、存爱, forming the foundation of Confucian political philosophy.
- [[li-ji-ben-sang-hurrying-to-mourning|Rushing to Mourning (Ben Sang) / 禮記·奔喪]] — The Book of Rites describes the ritual procedures for a son rushing home upon learning of a parent's death, including immediate response protocols, travel customs, and mourning ceremonies.
- [[li-ji-guan-yi-meaning-capping-rite|The Meaning of the Capping Rite / 禮記·冠義]] — A classical Confucian text from the Book of Rites explaining the ritual significance of the capping ceremony as the foundation of all ritual propriety and the gateway to adult moral responsibility.
- [[liji-aigong-wen-duke-ai-inquires|Duke Ai Inquires (Liji Chapter) / 禮記·哀公問]] — A Confucian dialogue between Duke Ai of Lu and Confucius on ritual propriety, governance, and the cultivation of the self through filial conduct.
- [[lijji-fang-ji-ritual-prevention|Fang Ji - Prevention Through Ritual in the Book of Rites / 禮記·坊記——以禮坊民]] — A classical Confucian chapter explaining how ritual propriety (禮) serves as a "dyke" (坊) to prevent moral corruption, social disorder, and improper conduct in human relations.
- [[ruxing-confucian-conduct|The Conduct of the Ru (Ruxing) / 儒行]] — A chapter from the Book of Rites presenting Confucius's dialogue with Duke Ai of Lu on the sixteen distinctive characteristics and virtues that define the Confucian scholar (儒).
- [[sang-da-ji-funeral-rituals-liji|Sang Da Ji: Major Records of Mourning / 喪大記：喪禮大典]] — A chapter from the Liji detailing hierarchical funeral procedures for rulers, ministers, and commoners, including encoffinement rites, mourning staff protocols, and deathbed rituals.
- [[six-mourning-principles-daxizhuan|The Six Principles of Mourning in Daxizhuan / 禮記大傳服術六法]] — The Daxizhuan enumerates six principles (服術) governing mourning garments and kinship obligations: 親親, 尊尊, 名, 出入, 長幼, 從服.
- [[three-day-encoffining-principle-liji-wensang|Three-Day Encoffining Principle / 三日而斂——三日乃斂之禮制]] — The Confucian principle of waiting three days before encoffining, based on the hope for the deceased's possible revival and the need for proper ritual preparation.
- [[wen-sang-mourning-staff-ji|The Mourning Staff in the Book of Rites / 問喪中的喪杖制度]] — A Confucian analysis of mourning ritual implements, particularly the mourning staff (杖) made from different materials for father and mother mourning, and the mourning cap (免).
- [[zhongni-yanji-ritual-teaching-liji|Zhongni at Home / 仲尼燕居]] — A chapter from the Book of Rites where Confucius discusses ritual propriety (禮), the principle of moderation (中), and the complementary roles of ritual and music (禮樂) with his disciples.

## Boshu-Version

- [[dao-de-jing-silk-manuscript|Dao De Jing (Silk Manuscript) / 道德經帛書本 / 道德経帛書本]] — The Dao De Jing Silk Manuscript presents the earliest complete version of Laozi's text, with its distinctive chapter ordering placing virtue before the Way.

## Brahma

- [[brahma-fourfold-teaching|Brahma's Fourfold Teaching on Liberation / 梵天王四法]] — Mahābrahmā's teachings on the four conditions for rebirth in the Brahma realm: non-self practice, one-pointedness of mind, compassionate liberation, and abandoning defilements.
