## Virtue

- [[beigongzi-ximenzi-virtue-fate-liezi|Beigongzi and Ximenzi — Virtue and Fate in Liezi / 北宮子與西門子：德厚命薄]] — A philosophical dialogue exploring how virtue (德) and fate (命) operate independently, with East Guo Master's revelation that Beigongzi has厚德薄命 while Ximenzi has厚命薄德.
- [[benevolence|Benevolence / 仁]] — Discusses the foundational role of benevolence in Confucian ethics and its implications for interpersonal relationships and governance.
- [[cai-quan-er-de-bu-xing|Complete Talent Without Displaying Virtue / 才全而德不形]] — A Zhuangzi philosophical concept about inner perfection transcending external form, exemplified by Ai Tai Tuo's effortless virtue.
- [[confucian-ethics|Confucian Ethics / 儒家伦理]] — This article explores the key concepts of Confucian ethics as presented in the Analects, focusing on filial piety and social conduct.
- [[extending-kindness-pushing-grace-tui-en|Extending Kindness (推恩) / 推恩]] — Mencius's doctrine that one should extend one's natural care from self and family to all people.
- [[governance-virtue-de|Governance through Virtue / 为政以德]] — Confucius's political philosophy emphasizing rule through moral virtue and ethical leadership rather than coercion or punishment.
- [[junzi|The Role of the Junzi / 君子的角色]] — Examines the characteristics and responsibilities of the Junzi in Confucian philosophy.
- [[mengzi-hegemon-true-king-ba-wang-distinction|Hegemon versus True King (霸道與王道) / 霸道與王道]] — Mencius's distinction between rulers who govern through force (hegemon) and those who rule through virtue (true king).
- [[ruxing-confucian-conduct|The Conduct of the Ru (Ruxing) / 儒行]] — A chapter from the Book of Rites presenting Confucius's dialogue with Duke Ai of Lu on the sixteen distinctive characteristics and virtues that define the Confucian scholar (儒).
- [[xiao-jing-classic-of-filial-piety|Classic of Filial Piety / 孝經]] — The Classic of Filial Piety (Xiaojing) is a Confucian text outlining the principles and practices of filial piety, existing in two main textual traditions.
- [[xing-hai-nei-wai|Inside and Outside the Physical Form / 形骸內外]] — A philosophical distinction in Zhuangzi between the physical body (form) and inner virtue, exemplified by deformed sages whose outer appearance differs from their inner perfection.
- [[仁爱|Ren'ai / 仁爱]] — The supreme virtue of humaneness and compassion in Confucian philosophy, representing the foundation of ethical behavior and ideal human relationships.
- [[君子|Junzi / 君子]] — The Confucian ideal of a morally cultivated person who embodies virtue and proper conduct.

## Virtue-Cultivation

- [[five-virtues-four-vices|Five Virtues and Four Vices / 五美四恶]] — Confucius's political philosophy outlining five virtues to cultivate and four vices to avoid in governance

## Virtue-Ethics

- [[analects-disciples|Disciples in the Analects / 论语弟子群像]] — Notable disciples appearing in Confucius's Analects
- [[benevolence|Benevolence / 仁]] — Discusses the foundational role of benevolence in Confucian ethics and its implications for interpersonal relationships and governance.
- [[benevolent-government-ren-zheng|Benevolent Government / 仁政 / 仁政]] — Mencius's political philosophy advocating that rulers should govern through benevolence and moral virtue rather than coercion and punishment.
- [[born-in-adversity-die-in-comfort|Born in Adversity, Die in Comfort / 生于忧患，死于安乐]] — Mencius' famous philosophical statement on how hardship builds character and virtue, while comfort leads to moral decline.
- [[confucian-sincerity-cheng|Sincerity (Cheng) in Confucianism / 誠]] — A core Confucian virtue representing authenticity, the metaphysical foundation of human nature, and the highest achievement of moral cultivation.
- [[confucius-analects|Analects of Confucius / 论语]] — A collection of sayings and ideas attributed to the Chinese philosopher Confucius.
- [[deben-cai-mo|Virtue as Root, Wealth as Branch / 德本財末]] — A Confucian economic ethics principle stating that virtue is the foundation of prosperity and that material wealth should never be prioritized over moral rectitude.
- [[doctrine-of-the-mean-zhongyong|Doctrine of the Mean (Zhongyong) / 中庸]] — A Confucian classic teaching the principle of balanced moderation and harmonious equilibrium as the foundation of virtue and sagehood.
- [[four-beginnings-si-duan|Four Beginnings / 四端 / 四端説]] — Mencius's theory that human nature contains four sprouts of virtue that can develop into benevolence, rightness, propriety, and wisdom.
- [[junzi-characteristics|Junzi Characteristics / 君子品格]] — Confucian ideal of the cultivated person who can be entrusted with governance and maintains integrity in crisis
- [[junzi|The Role of the Junzi / 君子的角色]] — Examines the characteristics and responsibilities of the Junzi in Confucian philosophy.
- [[mengzi-four-beginnings-siduan|Four Beginnings (四端) / 四端說]] — Mencius's doctrine that human nature contains four innate moral sprouts: compassion, shame, deference, and judgment.
- [[mengzi-righteousness-profit-debate|Mencius on Righteousness vs. Profit / 孟子论义利]] — Mencius' argument that rulers should be persuaded through benevolence and righteousness rather than profit, with profound implications for political philosophy.
- [[moral-cultivation-methods|Moral Cultivation Methods / 道德修养方法]] — Confucian techniques for character development including self-examination and principle adherence
- [[mozi-xiu-shen-self-cultivation|Mozi's Self-Cultivation / 墨子修身]] — A chapter from the Mozi discussing the fundamentals of self-cultivation, emphasizing courage, action, and integrity as the essential roots of moral cultivation.
- [[tian-xia-you-da-zun-san|Three Universally Honored Things / 天下有三尊]] — Mencius's doctrine that there are three things universally honored in the world: rank (position), age (seniority), and virtue (moral character), with implications for the hierarchy of honor in governance and society.
- [[yan-hui|Yan Hui / 颜回 / 顔回]] — Confucius's most beloved disciple, renowned for his wisdom, virtue, and contentment in poverty
- [[义|Righteousness / 义]] — A fundamental Confucian virtue representing moral righteousness, appropriate conduct, and the principle of doing what is right.
- [[仁愛|仁愛 / Benevolent Love]] — A Confucian virtue of genuine, substantive love for all people, foundational to moral cultivation and interpersonal harmony.
- [[仁者|仁者 / Benevolent Person]] — A person who has cultivated and embodies the Confucian virtue of benevolence (仁), serving as a moral exemplar.

## Virtue-Theory

- [[righteousness-internal-versus-external|Internal and External Righteousness / 仁內義外 / 仁内義外]] — The classical Confucian debate about whether righteousness (義) originates from within or is determined by external circumstances

## Virtue-Vs-Power

- [[wang-dao-true-king-hegemon|The Way of the True King vs. Hegemony (王道與霸道) / 王道と霸道]] — Mencius's distinction between righteous rule through virtue and coercive rule through force.

## Wang-Bi

- [[dao-the-way-taoism|Dao (The Way) / 道]] — The fundamental Taoist concept of Dao (The Way), the ultimate reality and principle underlying the universe.

## War-Criticism

- [[mozi-fei-gong-zhong-against-attack-middle|Mozi Fei Gong Zhong / 墨子·非攻中]] — Chapter 18 of the Mozi, continuing the critique of aggressive warfare with detailed economic analysis and historical examples demonstrating the futility of conquest.

## Warring States

- [[mozi-jie-zang-frugal-burial|Mozi on Frugal Burial (節葬) / 墨子節葬]] — Mozi's critique of excessive burial practices and prolonged mourning, arguing for simple burials based on the examples of ancient sage kings.
- [[mozi-seven-calamities-qi-huan|Seven Calamities in Mozi / 墨子七患]] — A Mohist analysis of the seven types of calamities that threaten state survival, emphasizing preparedness, agricultural foundation, and virtuous governance.

## Warring States Period

- [[mengzi|Mencius / 孟子]] — Mencius (c. 372–289 BCE) was the most prominent Confucian philosopher after Confucius, known for his doctrine of the innate goodness of human nature.

## Warring-States

- [[ding-fen-determining-names-positions|Determining Names and Positions (定分) / 定分]] — The Shang Yang doctrine establishing clear legal definitions of social roles and property rights as the foundation for orderly governance and the prevention of social chaos.
- [[mengzi-seven-chapters-gongsun-chou-part-two|Mencius: Gongsun Chou Part Two / 孟子·公孫丑下]] — A comprehensive wiki article covering the second chapter of Mencius, including analysis of its fourteen sections on political philosophy, governance principles, and historical events.
- [[mozi-fayi-standard-law|Mozi's Standard of Law (法儀) / 墨子法儀]] — Mozi argues that all governance requires a standard or model, and that Heaven (天) rather than parents, teachers, or rulers should serve as the ultimate ethical benchmark.
- [[mozi-gui-yi-valuing-righteousness|Mozi Chapter on Valuing Righteousness / 墨子·貴義]] — A chapter from the Mozi emphasizing righteousness as the highest value, contrasting with the utility of name-based versus actual selection, and demonstrating Mozi's political teachings through various encounters.
- [[mozi-illuminating-ghosts-ming-gui|Mozi - Illuminating Ghosts (Ming Gui) / 墨子·明鬼]] — Mozi's chapter arguing for the existence of ghosts and spirits who reward virtue and punish vice, using historical evidence and experiential verification.
- [[mozi-jie-zang-frugal-burial|Mozi on Frugal Burial (節葬) / 墨子節葬]] — Mozi's critique of excessive burial practices and prolonged mourning, arguing for simple burials based on the examples of ancient sage kings.
- [[mozi-seven-calamities-qi-huan|Seven Calamities in Mozi / 墨子七患]] — A Mohist analysis of the seven types of calamities that threaten state survival, emphasizing preparedness, agricultural foundation, and virtuous governance.
- [[mozi-upper-canon-jing-shang|Mozi Upper Canon (經上) / 墨子·經上]] — A foundational Mohist philosophical text defining core concepts including knowledge, virtue, cause, and methodology through systematic binary definitions.
- [[mozi-xiaoqu-mohist-logic|Mozi Xiaoqu: On Logical Reasoning / 墨子小取：論辯說之法]] — The Xiaoqu chapter of Mozi presents a systematic exposition of Mohist logic, including seven types of reasoning and epistemological principles for philosophical debate.
- [[ruo-min-weakening-the-people|Weakening the People (弱民) / 弱民]] — A Legalist political doctrine advocating that rulers should deliberately weaken the populace to strengthen state power, creating dependence through poverty and labor.
- [[shang-tong-upper-unity-mozi|Shang Tong (Upper Unity) / 尚同]] — Shang Tong is Mozi's political doctrine advocating hierarchical unity of thought under proper authority to achieve social order.

## Warring-States-Period

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-six-self-cultivation|Guanyinzi Chapter Six: Self-Cultivation and the Nature of Self / 關尹子第六章：修养与自我本性]] — Chapter Six of Guanyinzi explores the nature of self through dream analysis, form transformation, and the dissolution of subject-object distinctions, presenting core Daoist concepts of self-cultivation.
- [[mengzi|Mencius / 孟子]] — Mencius (c. 372–289 BCE) was the most prominent Confucian philosopher after Confucius, known for his doctrine of the innate goodness of human nature.
- [[mozi-banner-system-siege-defense|Mozi's Banner System for Siege Defense / 墨子旗幟制度]] — Mozi's systematic color-coded banner system for identifying defenders, materials, and military roles during siege warfare.
- [[mozi-da-qu|Mozi Da Qu (Great Adoption) / 墨子·大取]] — An analysis of the Mozi chapter "Da Qu" which discusses the methodology of moral weighing between benefit and harm, including principles of self-sacrifice for the world.
- [[mozi-jie-yong-utility-frugality|Mozi on Utility (節用) — The Principle of Frugality in Mohist Governance / 墨子節用思想——兼愛天下的功利原則]] — Mozi's "On Frugality" (節用) chapter outlines the principle that rulers should only employ resources for practical utility, eliminating wasteful expenditures to maximize social benefit and national strength.
- [[mozi-miscellaneous-defense-zashou|Miscellaneous Defense (雜守) / 墨子·雜守]] — A Mozi chapter detailing comprehensive urban defense strategies against siege warfare, covering tactical formations, garrison organization, supply management, and beacon communication systems.
- [[mozi-seven-calamities-qi-huan|Seven Calamities in Mozi / 墨子七患]] — A Mohist analysis of the seven types of calamities that threaten state survival, emphasizing preparedness, agricultural foundation, and virtuous governance.
- [[mozi-siege-defense-inventory|Mozi's Siege Defense Inventory Standards / 墨子守城物資儲備法]] — Standardized material requirements for city defense including provisions, weapons, and strategic supplies in Mozi's defensive doctrine.
- [[mozi-upper-canon-jing-shang|Mozi Upper Canon (經上) / 墨子·經上]] — A foundational Mohist philosophical text defining core concepts including knowledge, virtue, cause, and methodology through systematic binary definitions.
- [[mozi-xiaoqu-logical-methodology|Mozi's Logical Methodology / 墨子逻辑方法论 / 墨子論理方法論]] — A systematic analysis of the logical and dialectical methodology presented in the "Xiaoqu" chapter of Mozi, including the theory of names and realities, argumentation methods, and principles of correct reasoning.
- [[shang-tong-upper-unity-mozi|Shang Tong (Upper Unity) / 尚同]] — Shang Tong is Mozi's political doctrine advocating hierarchical unity of thought under proper authority to achieve social order.
- [[tu-men-tunnel-gates-city-defense|Tumen (Tunnel Gates) / 突門（城門突入口）]] — Tumen were hidden tunnel gate passages built into city walls at intervals of every hundred paces, designed as defensive traps against underground siege attacks using kilns, bellows, and smoke.
- [[zigong|Zigong / 子贡]] — A prominent disciple of Confucius known for diplomatic skill and commercial success.

## Water-Defense

- [[mozi-water-defense-strategies|Mozi's Water Defense Strategies / 墨子備水戰術]] — A chapter from the Mozi detailing defensive preparations and tactical responses against water attacks on fortified cities, including drainage systems, naval organization, and coordinated counterattacks.

## Water-Warfare

- [[mozi-water-defense-strategies|Mozi's Water Defense Strategies / 墨子備水戰術]] — A chapter from the Mozi detailing defensive preparations and tactical responses against water attacks on fortified cities, including drainage systems, naval organization, and coordinated counterattacks.

## Wedding-Ceremony

- [[liji-dahen-great-wedding-ritual|The Great Wedding Ritual (Da Hen) / 大昏——婚禮之道]] — Confucius's philosophy on the grand wedding ceremony (大昏) as the supreme form of reverence and the foundation of familial and political order.

## Welfare

- [[providing-people-sustenance-zhi-min-zhi-chan|Providing the People with Sustenance (制民之產) / 民に産業を制する]] — Mencius argued that benevolent government requires establishing the people's material welfare first—providing land, livestock, and education—because only those with stable livelihoods can develop constant virtue, making economic security the foundation of moral governance.

## Wenzi

- [[guarding-emptiness-void-wenzi-xu|Guarding Emptiness (守虛) in Wenzi / 文子"守虛"論]] — Guarding Emptiness (守虛) is the foundational Daoist practice in Wenzi Volume 3, emphasizing the cultivation of inner emptiness and receptivity as the gateway to understanding the Dao and achieving spiritual clarity.
- [[guarding-practices-wenzi-guardianship|Guarding Practices (守) in Wenzi / 文子"守"系列哲學]] — The ten "Guarding" practices in Wenzi Volume 3 represent a comprehensive Daoist self-cultivation methodology, including guarding emptiness, non-being, equilibrium, simplicity, clarity, authenticity, stillness, law, weakness, and plainness.
- [[guarding-stillness-wenzi-jing|Guarding Stillness (守靜) in Wenzi / 文子"守靜"論]] — Guarding Stillness (守靜) in Wenzi Volume 3 presents stillness as the fundamental governance principle, contrasting it with chaos caused by movement, and emphasizing its role in preserving spiritual energy and achieving longevity.
- [[wenzi-text|Wenzi (The Book of Master Wen) / 文子]] — An ancient Daoist text presenting Laozi's philosophy through a dialogue format, attributed to a disciple of Laozi, covering the nature of the Dao, non-action governance, and the ideal sage.

## Western-Jin

- [[deguang-taizi-jing-sutra|Sutra of Prince Deguang / 德光太子經]] — A Buddhist sutra recounting Prince Deguang's renunciation of royal comforts and his quest for Buddhist enlightenment, featuring teachings on the four pure practices and warnings against贪欲 and放逸.
- [[fa-ju-monk|Fajü (法炬) — Western Jin Buddhist Translator / 法炬]] — Fajü was a Buddhist monk from the Western Jin dynasty who translated multiple sutras including the Sutra of the Three Cycles of Past Lives.
- [[qianshi-san-zhuan-jing|Sutra of the Three Cycles of Past Lives / 前世三轉經]] — A Buddhist sutra translated by Faju during the Western Jin dynasty, narrating the Buddha's past life stories of self-sacrifice and the merit of giving, including the breast-milk offering and tiger-feeding incidents.
- [[sheng-jing-birth-sutras|Sheng Jing (Birth Sutras) / 生經]] — A Buddhist sutra collection translated by Zhu Fahu during the Western Jin dynasty, containing ten narratives illustrating karmic principles and monastic discipline.

## Wheel-Turning-King

- [[ding-sheng-wang-king-caitanya-dhrtarastra|King Ding Sheng (Ding Sheng Wang) / 頂生王]] — King Ding Sheng is a bodhisattva incarnation who was born from his father's crown, practiced generous giving, conquered the four continents, and ascended through the thirty-three heavens, illustrating Buddhist teachings on merit and the dangers of greed.

## Wheeled-Vehicles

- [[fenyun-covered-siege-vehicle|Fenyun Covered Siege Vehicle / 轒轀]] — A heavily armored wheeled vehicle used in ancient Chinese siege warfare for crossing water obstacles and breaching defensive works, documented in Mozi's water defense chapter.

## Will

- [[zhi-qing-relation|Will and Passion-Nature / 志與情的關係]] — Mencius's doctrine on the hierarchical yet interactive relationship between will (zhi) and passion-nature (qing)

## Wisdom

- [[bian-shi-wei-zhi|Transforming Knowledge into Wisdom / 變識為智]] — The Daoist philosophical concept of transforming rigid consciousness (識) into flexible wisdom (智), a key theme in Guanyinzi Chapter 5.
- [[entry-all-knowledge-dharani-gate|入一切種智行陀羅尼門 / Entry into the All-Knowledge Dhāraṇī Gate]] — A profound Mahāyāna dhāraṇī practice for entering complete wisdom, enabling practitioners to obtain countless samādhis,Store dhāraṇīs, and achieve supreme enlightenment.
- [[five-roots-five-powers-an-shigao-commentary|Five Roots and Five Powers / 五根五力]] — The Five Roots (信進念定慧) and Five Powers (信力精進力念力定力慧力) as expounded in An Shigao's Han Dynasty Buddhist commentary.
- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-nine-medicinal-treatment|Guanyinzi Chapter Nine: Medicinal Treatment / 關尹子第九篇：藥]] — Chapter 9 of Guanyinzi presents teachings on miscellaneous remedies for spiritual and moral ailments, addressing the proper balance between wisdom, speech, and action through paradoxes and practical metaphors.
- [[jiu-fang-gao-horse-divination-liezi|Jiu Fang Gao's Horse Divination / 九方皋相馬]] — A parable about seeing essential nature beyond superficial appearance, exemplified through horse divination that prioritizes inner truth over external signs.
- [[the-lost-sheep-at-the-crossroads|The Lost Sheep at the Crossroads / 歧路亡羊]] — A philosophical parable illustrating how multiple paths lead to loss, emphasizing the importance of unified focus in learning and practice.
- [[understanding-heaven-knowing-fate-liezi|Understanding Heaven and Knowing Fate (樂天知命) / 樂天知命]] — A Liezi philosophical concept about rejoicing in Heaven's will and knowing one's fate, evolving from conventional wisdom to transcendent understanding.

## Wisdom-Limits

- [[shen-gui-dream-yuan-jun-zhuangzi|The Divine Turtle Story / 神龜能見夢於元君]] — The parable of the divine turtle that could appear in dreams but could not avoid the net, illustrating the limits of wisdom and foreknowledge.

## Woman-Teaching

- [[seven-types-wives-buddhist-teaching|Seven Types of Wives in Buddhist Teaching / 七輩婦]] — A Buddhist typology of seven wife archetypes from the Sutra on Yù Yě Nǚ, ranging from the ideal Mother-wife to the demonic Murderer-wife.

## Womens-Education

- [[confucian-gender-roles-neize-education|Gender-Specific Education in the Rites / 男女之教]] — Confucian gender-differentiated education practices, with boys learning the Six Arts and girls learning women's affairs for their designated domestic and social roles.

## Womens-Studies

- [[seven-types-wives-buddhist-teaching|Seven Types of Wives in Buddhist Teaching / 七輩婦]] — A Buddhist typology of seven wife archetypes from the Sutra on Yù Yě Nǚ, ranging from the ideal Mother-wife to the demonic Murderer-wife.

## World-Origin

- [[light-sound-heaven-buddhist-cosmology|Light-Sound Heaven / 光音天]] — The second dhyana heaven in Buddhist cosmology from which beings descended to earth according to the White Cloth Golden Banner Sutra.

## Worldly Recognition

- [[liezi-journey-early-return|Liezi's Journey and Early Return / 列禦寇之齊與伯昏瞀人的故事]] — The story of Liezi journeying to Qi but turning back after becoming "startled" by receiving special treatment, illustrating the danger of being sought after by people.

## Worthy-Scholars

- [[mozi-qin-shi-cherishing-scholars|Mozi Chapter on Cherishing Scholars / 墨子親士]] — A chapter from the Mozi emphasizing that rulers must cherish and draw close to worthy scholars to preserve their states and achieve success.

## Wu-Pu

- [[wu-pu-five-animal-play-hua-tuo|Wu Pu and the Five Animal Play / 吴普与五禽戏]] — Wu Pu, student of physician Hua Tuo, practiced the Five Animal Play (Wu Qin Xi) and achieved longevity over 100 years, demonstrating the health benefits of Daoist physical cultivation.

## Wu-Wei

- [[guanyinzi-chapter-nine|Guanyinzi Chapter Nine: On Medicine / 關尹子第九章：藥者雜治]] — Chapter 9 of Guanyinzi presents the "medicine" metaphor for spiritual practice, discussing how the sage applies various remedies to correct imbalances between extremes.
- [[guanyinzi-chapter-six-self-transformation|Guanyinzi Chapter Six: On the Self / 關尹子第六章：論自我]] — Chapter 6 of Guanyinzi explores the nature of self, dream versus waking states, form transformation, and the concept of no-self (無我) in Daoist philosophy.
- [[huaxu-kingdom-liezi|The Huaxu Kingdom / 華胥氏之國]] — The Yellow Emperor's dream of the utopian Huaxu Kingdom and his subsequent enlightened reign, illustrating the Daoist ideal of natural governance through non-action.
- [[non-attachment-guanyinzi|Non-Attachment (無物) in Guanyinzi / 關尹子之無物論]] — The Guanyinzi's doctrine of non-attachment to things (無物), emphasizing that the sage responds to the world without grasping and nurtures the self without ego.
- [[zhuangzi-gengsang-chu-chapter|Gengsang Chu Chapter (庚桑楚) / 庚桑楚篇]] — Chapter 23 of Zhuangzi featuring the disciple Gengsang Chu's philosophy of self-preservation, his dialogue with Laozi on the "hygiene of life," and profound discussions of being and non-being.
- [[zhuangzi-heaven-earth-chapter|Zhuangzi Chapter on Heaven and Earth / 莊子·天地]] — A key chapter from Zhuangzi exploring the nature of the Way, governance through non-action, and the corruption of innate nature through artificiality.
- [[zhuangzi-supreme-joy-zhi-le|Supreme Joy (至樂) in Zhuangzi / 莊子至樂篇]] — Zhuangzi's philosophical exploration of true happiness beyond worldly pleasures, arguing that ultimate joy transcends conventional notions of joy itself.
- [[zhuangzi-turtle-story-rejection|Zhuangzi's Turtle Story / 莊子曳尾塗中]] — The famous anecdote of Zhuangzi refusing official service from the King of Chu, choosing instead to drag his tail in the mud—a classic expression of Daoist values.

## Wuliangshou-Jing

- [[virama-bodhisattva-pusajiamo|Vīrāma (毘羅摩) — Bodhisattva of Giving]] — A minister's son who became chief minister and exemplified the perfection of giving through vast generosity and skillful means.

## Wuwei

- [[zhuangzi-ke-yi-chapter|Kè Yì (Cultivating Deliberate Purpose) / 刻意]] — Zhuangzi Chapter 15 explores five types of self-cultivation and advocates for the sage's path of tranquility, simplicity, and non-deliberate action.

## Wuxing

- [[five-elements-theory-constancy-transformation|Five Elements Theory: Constancy and Transformation / 五行常變論]] — The theory of five elements (water, fire, wood, metal, earth) as constant yet infinitely variable principles in Chinese cosmology and philosophy.

## Wuyue

- [[five-peaks-true-form-map-wuyue-zhenxing-tu|Five Peaks True Form Map / 五嶽真形圖]] — A most sacred Taoist talisman text described in Baopuzi as requiring blood oaths, forty-year transmission cycles, and possessing powers to ward off evil, heal diseases, cross waters, and reveal hidden knowledge.

## Xiao

- [[three-years-mourning|Three Years Mourning / 三年之丧]] — The traditional mourning period of three years emphasizes filial piety and respect for deceased parents.

## Yakṣa

- [[sakra-tests-king-subhuti-yaksha|Śakra's Test of King Subhūti / 帝釋試験妙色王]] — The narrative episode where Śakra (Indra), disguised as a yakṣa, tests King Subhūti's devotion to the Dharma by demanding his son, wife, and body as offerings.

## Yan-Zi

- [[mozi-rejecting-confucians-fei-ru|Mozi's Rejection of Confucians (非儒下) / 墨子·非儒下]] — A Mohist critique of Confucian ethics, mourning rituals, fatalism, and Confucius's political conduct through detailed arguments and historical anecdotes.

## Yang-Zhu

- [[yang-zhu-philosophy-liezi|Yang Zhu's Philosophy in Liezi / 楊朱篇的哲學思想]] — Yang Zhu's individualist and hedonist philosophy as presented in the Liezi, emphasizing self-preservation, the brevity of life, and rejection of fame and social obligations.

## Yellow-Emperor

- [[huaxu-dream-liezi|The Huaxu Dream (華胥之夢) / 華胥之夢]] — The Yellow Emperor's dream journey to the utopian land of Huaxu, representing ideal governance and natural harmony.
- [[huaxu-kingdom-liezi|The Huaxu Kingdom / 華胥氏之國]] — The Yellow Emperor's dream of the utopian Huaxu Kingdom and his subsequent enlightened reign, illustrating the Daoist ideal of natural governance through non-action.

## Yellow-White-Alchemy

- [[baopuzi-yellow-white-alchemy|Baopuzi on Yellow and White Alchemy / 抱朴子論黃白之術]] — Volume 16 of Ge Hong's Baopuzi presents a systematic exposition of 黄白术 (transmutation alchemy), defending its legitimacy and providing detailed alchemical formulas for creating gold and silver.

## Yijing

- [[bagua-formation-trigrams-yijing|Eight Trigrams Formation / 八卦成列]] — The philosophical principle of how the eight trigrams are arranged to manifest all phenomena in the Yijing
- [[miaose-wang-bodhisattva-sacrifice|King Subhūti / 妙色王]] — A past incarnation of the Buddha who sacrificed his son, wife, and body to obtain the Dharma teaching on attachment.
- [[miaose-wang-yinyuan-jing|King Subhūti's Cause and Conditions Sutra / 妙色王因緣經]] — A Buddhist sutra depicting King Subhūti's supreme sacrifice of his son, wife, and body to obtain the Dharma teaching on attachment.

## Yijing-Translation

- [[you-ai-gu-sheng-you-you-yin-ai-gu-sheng-kong|From Love Arises Sorrow; From Love Arises Fear / 由愛故生憂 由愛故生怖]] — A canonical Buddhist gatha stating that attachment is the origin of suffering and fear, and liberation comes from detachment.

## Yin Xi

- [[關尹子|關尹子 / Guanyinzi]] — A Daoist text attributed to Yin Xi, presenting teachings on sage governance and cosmic transformation

## Yinwen

- [[laochengzi-illusion-transformation-liezi|Lao Chengzi's Study of Illusion / 老成子學幻 / 老成子の幻化学]] — The philosophical narrative of Lao Chengzi learning the art of transformation and illusion from Yin Wen, including the Daoist theory of change and the nature of reality.

## Yue-Ming-Puti-Jing

- [[si-yuan-four-vows-bodhisattva-practice-yue-ming-jing|Four Vows (四願) in the Moonlight Bodhisattva Sutra / 月明童子經四願——菩薩修行之根本誓願]] — The Four Vows are the fundamental commitments bodhisattvas make for spiritual practice, encompassing skillful means, spiritual friendship, giving, and dharma practice.
- [[yue-ming-tongzi-moonlight-youth-bodhisattva-sutra|Moonlight Youth (月明童子) — Protagonist of the Moonlight Bodhisattva Sutra / 月明童子——《月光菩薩經》的主人公]] — The Moonlight Youth (月明童子) is a clean-minded young man addressed by the Buddha, symbolizing purity of mind in the sutra.

## Yuye-Sutra

- [[seven-types-wives-buddhist-teaching|Seven Types of Wives in Buddhist Teaching / 七輩婦]] — A Buddhist typology of seven wife archetypes from the Sutra on Yù Yě Nǚ, ranging from the ideal Mother-wife to the demonic Murderer-wife.

## Zen

- [[さとり|Satori / 悟]] — Satori is a Japanese Buddhist term for a sudden awakening or enlightenment experience, often associated with Zen practice.

## Zhang-Cang

- [[liang-zhang-min-zhang-cang-lactotherapy|Zhang Cang's Lactotherapy / 张苍乳汁养生]] — Zhang Cang (张苍), Han Dynasty Chancellor, achieved exceptional longevity (180 years) through lactotherapy (吮妇人乳汁), demonstrating Daoist practices in historical records.

## Zhang-Liang

- [[zhang-liang-daoist-cultivation-exile|Zhang Liang's Daoist Cultivation and Exile / 张良修道绝谷]] — Zhang Liang (Zhang Liang), famous strategist of the Han founding, abandoned worldly affairs to pursue Daoist cultivation and practices of fasting (绝谷), demonstrating historical evidence for immortality cultivation.

## Zhiren

- [[perfect-person-zhiren-zhuangzi|Perfect Person (Zhiren) / 至人]] — The highest ideal in Daoist cultivation - one who attains utmost beauty and joy by wandering freely beyond all transformations and attachments.

## Zhongyong

- [[cai-yu-bu-cai-zhi-jian|Between Being Useful and Useless / 材與不材之間]] — A Zhuangzi philosophy on navigating between utility and uselessness through alignment with the Dao, avoiding the pitfalls of both extremes.
- [[doctrine-of-the-mean-zhongyong|Doctrine of the Mean (Zhongyong) / 中庸]] — A Confucian classic teaching the principle of balanced moderation and harmonious equilibrium as the foundation of virtue and sagehood.

## Zhou-Dynasty

- [[preventing-speech-damming-rivers|Preventing People's Speech is Harder than Damming Rivers / 防民之口甚於防川]] — Duke Shao's famous political metaphor warning that suppressing criticism is more dangerous than damming rivers.

## Zhousongwang

- [[woodcutter-dream-deer-liezi|The Woodcutter's Dream of the Deer / 薪者夢鹿]] — A philosophical story from Liezi about a woodcounter who dreams of catching a deer, leading to a legal dispute about the nature of reality and dream.

## Zhu-Fahu

- [[deguang-taizi-jing-sutra|Sutra of Prince Deguang / 德光太子經]] — A Buddhist sutra recounting Prince Deguang's renunciation of royal comforts and his quest for Buddhist enlightenment, featuring teachings on the four pure practices and warnings against贪欲 and放逸.
- [[sheng-jing-birth-sutras|Sheng Jing (Birth Sutras) / 生經]] — A Buddhist sutra collection translated by Zhu Fahu during the Western Jin dynasty, containing ten narratives illustrating karmic principles and monastic discipline.

## Zhu-Xi

- [[great-learning-daxue|The Great Learning / 大學]] — A foundational Confucian classic on self-cultivation and the investigation of things, forming part of the Four Books of Confucianism.

## Zhuangzi

- [[bo-chengzi-gao-zhuangzi|Bo Chengzi Gao / 伯成子高]] — A hermit from the Zhuangzi who refused to serve as a feudal lord, critiquing the shift from reward-free governance to punitive systems.
- [[cai-quan-er-de-bu-xing|Complete Talent Without Displaying Virtue / 才全而德不形]] — A Zhuangzi philosophical concept about inner perfection transcending external form, exemplified by Ai Tai Tuo's effortless virtue.
- [[cai-yu-bu-cai-zhi-jian|Between Being Useful and Useless / 材與不材之間]] — A Zhuangzi philosophy on navigating between utility and uselessness through alignment with the Dao, avoiding the pitfalls of both extremes.
- [[chongyan-revered-discourse-zhuangzi|Chongyan (Revered Discourse) in Zhuangzi / 重言（莊子）]] — "Revered Discourse" (重言) — the second of Zhuangzi's three discourse modes, quoting ancient sages and elders to lend authority to philosophical teachings.
- [[de-yi-wang-yan-zhuangzi|Forget the Words When the Meaning is Understood / 得意忘言]] — Zhuangzi's philosophical principle that true understanding transcends language, illustrated through the fish trap and rabbit snare metaphors.
- [[gengsang-chu-zhuangzi|Gengsang Chu Chapter in Zhuangzi / 莊子·庚桑楚]] — A chapter in Zhuangzi featuring the disciple Gengsang Chu's philosophy of preserving life, avoiding fame, and the Daoist concept of the "hygiene classic."
- [[hao-bridge-debate-fish-happiness|The Hao Bridge Debate / 濠梁之辯]] — The famous philosophical exchange between Zhuangzi and Hui Zi about whether one can know a fish's happiness, exploring questions of consciousness and knowledge.
- [[hu-zi-huzi-zhuangzi|Huzi (壺子) - The Master of Hidden Virtue / 壺子]] — The Daoist master Huzi who demonstrated four different spiritual manifestations to the fortune-teller Jixian, illustrating the illusory nature of external judgments and the depth of inner cultivation.
- [[hubin-wheelmaking-discourse-zhuangzi|Wheelwright's Wheel-Making Discourse / 輪扁斲輪]] — A philosophical discourse in Zhuangzi where a wheelwright explains to Duke Huan why the words of the sages are merely "dregs" and true wisdom cannot be transmitted through language.
- [[hun-tun-chaos-death-zhuangzi|The Death of Hun-tun (渾沌之死) / 渾沌之死]] — The parable in Zhuangzi where well-intentioned intervention by 儵 and 忽 destroys the natural simplicity of 渾沌, illustrating the dangers of artificial interference with natural states.
- [[jian-de-zhi-guo|Nation of Established Virtue / 建德之國]] — The ideal Daoist society described by Shi Nan Yi Liao in the Zhuangzi—simple, without artificial desires, where people act freely and live and die with peace.
- [[nanbo-ziqi-form-dead-wood-heart-dead-ashes|Nanbo Ziqi / 南伯子綦]] — A legendary Daoist sage from Zhuangzi described as sitting in meditation with "form like withered bones and heart like dead ashes," symbolizing profound spiritual realization.
- [[qiushui-autumn-floods-zhuangzi|Autumn Floods / 秋水]] — Chapter 17 of Zhuangzi featuring the philosophical dialogue between the River God and Sea God on relativism, perspective, and the limits of human understanding.
- [[remove-harmful-horses-governance|Remove the Harmful Horses / 去其害馬]] — A governance principle from Zhuangzi where the Yellow Emperor learns from a child that ruling the world is like tending horses—simply remove what harms them.
- [[shan-mu-yi-dai-niao|The Yi Dai Bird / 意怠鳥]] — The story of the humble, non-competitive Yi Dai bird in Zhuangzi's Mountain Trees chapter, illustrating the Daoist path of safety through humility and non-distinction.
- [[shen-gui-dream-yuan-jun-zhuangzi|The Divine Turtle Story / 神龜能見夢於元君]] — The parable of the divine turtle that could appear in dreams but could not avoid the net, illustrating the limits of wisdom and foreknowledge.
- [[tian-yun-zhuangzi|Heaven's Operations / 天運]] — A philosophical exploration of the spontaneous operations of Heaven and cosmic process in Chapter 14 of Zhuangzi, addressing governance, music, and the nature of the Way.
- [[wuyong-zhi-yong-usefulness-of-uselessness|The Usefulness of Uselessness / 無用之用]] — A central Zhuangzi parable about the useless tree that survives because it has no use to humans, illustrating the Daoist principle that apparent uselessness may be the highest utility.
- [[xiang-tian-shu-he-xiang-tian-shu-zhi-jian|Natural Affinity versus Interest-Based Relationships / 相天屬與相利合]] — A philosophical contrast in Zhuangzi between relationships formed through natural bonds versus those formed through mutual benefit.
- [[xing-hai-nei-wai|Inside and Outside the Physical Form / 形骸內外]] — A philosophical distinction in Zhuangzi between the physical body (form) and inner virtue, exemplified by deformed sages whose outer appearance differs from their inner perfection.
- [[xinzhai-heart-fasting|Heart Fasting / 心齋]] — A Zhuangzi concept of spiritual purification through emptiness of the heart-mind, taught by Confucius to Yan Hui as the path to navigate the human world.
- [[xu-ji-yi-you-shi|Swimming Through the World with an Empty Self / 虛己以遊世]] — A Zhuangzi parable about how one who empties oneself of ego and self-interest becomes invulnerable to external harm.
- [[zhihou-yan-chengzi-spiritual-progression-zhuangzi|Yan Chengzi's Nine-Year Spiritual Progression / 顏成子游九年大妙]] — The account of Yan Chengzi You's nine-year spiritual transformation from worldly refinement to ultimate enlightenment, as recorded in Zhuangzi.
- [[zhili-qixing-deformed-ones-zhuangzi|The Deformed Ones / 支離其形]] — Zhuangzi's celebration of physically deformed individuals who, excluded from worldly usefulness, survive and prosper—the paradox of form versus spirit in Daoist philosophy.
- [[zhuangzi-daozhi-chapter|Robber Zhi Chapter (盜跖) / 盜跖篇]] — Zhuangzi Chapter 29 presents a fictional dialogue between Confucius and Robber Zhi, featuring radical critiques of Confucian morality, civilization, and social order.
- [[zhuangzi-fables-parables-yuyan|Zhuangzi Chapter 27: Fables / 莊子·寓言]] — The 27th chapter of Zhuangzi introducing three types of discourse—fables, elders' sayings, and spontaneous speech—to convey Daoist teachings through external examples and natural patterns.
- [[zhuangzi-fisherman-yu-fu|The Fisherman Chapter (Yu Fu) / 莊子·漁父]] — A Zhuangzi chapter featuring a Daoist fisherman who teaches Confucius about authenticity (真), criticizing artificial ritual and social conventions while advocating naturalness and genuine sincerity.
- [[zhuangzi-gengsang-chu-chapter|Gengsang Chu Chapter (庚桑楚) / 庚桑楚篇]] — Chapter 23 of Zhuangzi featuring the disciple Gengsang Chu's philosophy of self-preservation, his dialogue with Laozi on the "hygiene of life," and profound discussions of being and non-being.
- [[zhuangzi-great-skill-appears-clumsy-da-qiao-ruo-zhuo|"Great Skill Appears Clumsy" / 大巧若拙]] — A famous Zhuangzi paradox stating that true mastery looks awkward or clumsy, while apparent skill invites theft and competition.
- [[zhuangzi-heaven-earth-chapter|Zhuangzi Chapter on Heaven and Earth / 莊子·天地]] — A key chapter from Zhuangzi exploring the nature of the Way, governance through non-action, and the corruption of innate nature through artificiality.
- [[zhuangzi-ke-yi-chapter|Kè Yì (Cultivating Deliberate Purpose) / 刻意]] — Zhuangzi Chapter 15 explores five types of self-cultivation and advocates for the sage's path of tranquility, simplicity, and non-deliberate action.
- [[zhuangzi-shanxing-chapter|Shanxing (Rectifying Nature) Chapter in Zhuangzi / 莊子·繕性]] — A chapter discussing the rectification of nature through stillness and naturalness, contrasting worldly learning with returning to original spontaneity, and analyzing the historical decline of the Dao.
- [[zhuangzi-supreme-joy-zhi-le|Supreme Joy (至樂) in Zhuangzi / 莊子至樂篇]] — Zhuangzi's philosophical exploration of true happiness beyond worldly pleasures, arguing that ultimate joy transcends conventional notions of joy itself.
- [[zhuangzi-thieving-box-qu-qie|Zhuangzi's "Thieving the Box" / 莊子·胠篋]] — A Zhuangzi chapter arguing that preventive measures against petty theft enable great thieves, and that sage teachings are exploited by those who steal states.
- [[zhuangzi-turtle-story-rejection|Zhuangzi's Turtle Story / 莊子曳尾塗中]] — The famous anecdote of Zhuangzi refusing official service from the King of Chu, choosing instead to drag his tail in the mud—a classic expression of Daoist values.
- [[zhuangzi-ying-di-wang|Zhuangzi Chapter 7: Responses of Kings / 莊子·應帝王]] — Chapter 7 of Zhuangzi discusses the qualities of the ideal ruler and the principle of non-action in governance, featuring the famous allegory of Chaos (渾沌).
- [[zhuangzi-zhi-bei-you-chapter|Zhi Bei You (Northern Journey of Knowledge) / 知北遊]] — Chapter 22 of Zhuangzi exploring the nature of knowledge and the omnipresence of the Dao through dialogues between Zhi, Wu Wei Wei, Kuang Qu, and Huang Di.
- [[子貢|Zigong / 子貢]] — A prominent disciple of Confucius known for diplomatic skill and commercial acumen.

## Zhufahu

- [[sheng-jing-birth-sutras|Sheng Jing (Birth Sutras) / 生經]] — A Buddhist sutra collection translated by Zhu Fahu during the Western Jin dynasty, containing ten narratives illustrating karmic principles and monastic discipline.

## Ziqi

- [[nanbo-ziqi-form-dead-wood-heart-dead-ashes|Nanbo Ziqi / 南伯子綦]] — A legendary Daoist sage from Zhuangzi described as sitting in meditation with "form like withered bones and heart like dead ashes," symbolizing profound spiritual realization.

## Ziran

- [[ji-shengzi-fighting-roosters-liezi|Ji Shengzi's Fighting Roosters: Spiritual Mastery / 紀渻子養鬭雞 — 精神修養之道]] — The story of Ji Shengzi cultivating fighting roosters illustrates the Daoist path to spiritual mastery through the progression from false confidence to true tranquility.

## Zixia

- [[confucius-at-leisure-liji|Confucius at Leisure / 孔子閒居]] — A Confucian dialogue between Confucius and Zixia on the qualities of an ideal ruler, exploring the Five Arrivals, Three "Not-Haves," and the Three Selflessnesses.

## Āgama Literature

- [[four-āgama-commentary-ā-hán-mù-chao-jiě|四阿鋡暮抄解 / Four Āgama Commentary (Ā Hán Mù Chao Jiě)]] — A Buddhist commentary on the Four Āgama text, presenting a systematic analysis of the "Three Factors" framework for Buddhist practice and liberation.

## Śrāvakayāna

- [[four-āgama-commentary-ā-hán-mù-chao-jiě|四阿鋡暮抄解 / Four Āgama Commentary (Ā Hán Mù Chao Jiě)]] — A Buddhist commentary on the Four Āgama text, presenting a systematic analysis of the "Three Factors" framework for Buddhist practice and liberation.

## Śakra

- [[thirty-three-heavens|Trayastriṃśa Heaven / 三十三天（忉利天）]] — The Heaven of Thirty-Three Gods where the initial discourse of the Great Firm Brāhmaṇa Sūtra takes place, presided over by Śakra (帝釋天主) and the site of the first teaching of the eight rare qualities.

## Śibi-Jataka

- [[king-sibi-pigeon-sacrifice|King Śibi Saves the Pigeon (尸毘王救鴿) - The Great Sacrifice of Flesh for Life]] — The classic Jataka tale of King Śibi who cut off his own flesh to save a pigeon from a hawk, exemplifying the perfection of giving in Buddhism.

## Śrāvaka

- [[five-ascetics-seven-śrāvaka-buddhist-narrative|五仙人的故事與七覺支的象徵意義 / 五仙人故事與七覺支象徵意義 / 五仙人の物語と七覚支の象徴的意義]] — The narrative of the five ascetics in the Shengjing, illustrating Buddhist teachings on karma, rebirth, and the development of the seven factors of awakening.

## Śrāvaka-Path

- [[nikuda-brahmin-sutra|Discourse on Nikuda Brahmins / 佛說尼拘陀梵志經]] — A Buddhist sutra depicting Buddha's dialogue with Nikuda brahmins, critiquing external ascetic practices and revealing defilement accumulation.

## 五行

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-spirit-five-elements|Guan Yinzi Chapter 4: On Spirit and the Five Elements / 關尹子第四章：論精神與五行]] — Chapter 4 of Guan Yinzi explores the nature of essence (精), spirit (神), ethereal soul (魂), and corporeal soul (魄), examining how the Five Elements correspond to human consciousness and the philosophical implications for understanding life, death, and unity with the cosmos.

## 佛教故事

- [[prince-sujata-story-bodhisattva-sacrifice|The Story of Prince Sūjāta (須闍提太子) - Buddhist Narrative of Self-Sacrifice]] — The narrative of Prince Sūjāta who offered his own flesh to feed his starving parents, serving as the central exemplum of filial piety and compassionate self-sacrifice in Mahayana Buddhism.

## 儒家軍事思想

- [[sima-fa-methods-of-the-marshal|Methods of the Marshal / 司馬法]] — A classical Chinese military treatise from the Warring States period, emphasizing benevolent warfare, ritual propriety in military affairs, and the integration of Confucian ethics with military strategy.

## 先秦兵學

- [[wu-zi|Wu Zi / 吳子]] — A classical Chinese military treatise by Wu Qi, presenting principles of state governance, military strategy, troop discipline, and leadership through dialogues with King Wu of Wei.

## 先秦兵法

## 兵家

- [[wu-zi|Wu Zi / 吳子]] — A classical Chinese military treatise by Wu Qi, presenting principles of state governance, military strategy, troop discipline, and leadership through dialogues with King Wu of Wei.

## 兼愛

- [[jian-ai|Universal Love (兼愛) / 兼愛]] — The Mohist principle advocating equal care for all people without distinction, considered the foundation of Mozi's ethical philosophy.

## 军事防御

## 城防

## 墨子

- [[mozi-xiaoqu-mohist-logic|Mozi Xiaoqu: On Logical Reasoning / 墨子小取：論辯說之法]] — The Xiaoqu chapter of Mozi presents a systematic exposition of Mohist logic, including seven types of reasoning and epistemological principles for philosophical debate.

## 孝道

- [[prince-sujata-story-bodhisattva-sacrifice|The Story of Prince Sūjāta (須闍提太子) - Buddhist Narrative of Self-Sacrifice]] — The narrative of Prince Sūjāta who offered his own flesh to feed his starving parents, serving as the central exemplum of filial piety and compassionate self-sacrifice in Mahayana Buddhism.

## 守

- [[guarding-practices-wenzi-guardianship|Guarding Practices (守) in Wenzi / 文子"守"系列哲學]] — The ten "Guarding" practices in Wenzi Volume 3 represent a comprehensive Daoist self-cultivation methodology, including guarding emptiness, non-being, equilibrium, simplicity, clarity, authenticity, stillness, law, weakness, and plainness.

## 守一

- [[shou-yi-zhi-dao-guarding-the-one|Guarding the One (守一之道) / 守一之道]] — A fundamental Daoist practice of maintaining singular focus on the Dao for immortality, protection, and spiritual transformation, as described in Baopuzi.

## 守虛

- [[guarding-emptiness-void-wenzi-xu|Guarding Emptiness (守虛) in Wenzi / 文子"守虛"論]] — Guarding Emptiness (守虛) is the foundational Daoist practice in Wenzi Volume 3, emphasizing the cultivation of inner emptiness and receptivity as the gateway to understanding the Dao and achieving spiritual clarity.

## 守靜

- [[guarding-stillness-wenzi-jing|Guarding Stillness (守靜) in Wenzi / 文子"守靜"論]] — Guarding Stillness (守靜) in Wenzi Volume 3 presents stillness as the fundamental governance principle, contrasting it with chaos caused by movement, and emphasizing its role in preserving spiritual energy and achieving longevity.

## 抱朴子

- [[baopuzi-immortality-debate|Bao Puzi on Immortals / 抱朴子论神仙不死]] — Ge Hong's philosophical defense of immortality through natural transformation and historical evidence.
- [[baopuzi-yellow-white-alchemy|Baopuzi on Yellow and White Alchemy / 抱朴子論黃白之術]] — Volume 16 of Ge Hong's Baopuzi presents a systematic exposition of 黄白术 (transmutation alchemy), defending its legitimacy and providing detailed alchemical formulas for creating gold and silver.

## 柱者建天地

- [[guanyinzi-chapter-two|Guanyinzi Chapter Two / 關尹子第二章]] — Chapter Two of the Daoist classic Guanyinzi explores the cosmological principles of establishing heaven and earth through all things, the interplay of essence, spirit, and the five elements.

## 爾雅

- [[erya-classic-chinese-glossary|Erya (Classic of Correct Rhymes) / 爾雅]] — The Erya is China's oldest comprehensive glossary, compiled during the Warring States-Han period, providing definitions of classical terms across categories with phonetic annotations.

## 生成

- [[transformation-generation-huasheng-liezi|Transformation and Generation (化生) / 化生]] — The Liezi philosophical principle that all things are generated through transformation in an endless cyclical process.

## 生死

## 神

- [[guan-yinzi-chapter-four-essence-spirit|Essence and Spirit (精神) in Guǎn Yǐnzǐ Chapter 4 / 關尹子第四章：精與神]] — A comprehensive analysis of the Daoist concepts of jīng (essence) and shén (spirit) as discussed in Chapter 4 of Guǎn Yǐnzǐ, examining their relationship to the Five Elements and the philosophical principle of "no-self" (wú wǒ).

## 魏文侯

- [[wu-zi|Wu Zi / 吳子]] — A classical Chinese military treatise by Wu Qi, presenting principles of state governance, military strategy, troop discipline, and leadership through dialogues with King Wu of Wei.
